It is truth universally acknowledged, that single man in possession
of good fortune, must be in want of wife.

However little known the feelings or views of such man may be on his
first entering neighbourhood, this truth is so well fixed in the minds
of the surrounding families, that he is considered as the rightful
property of some one or other of their daughters.

"My dear Mr. —," said his lady to him one day, "have you heard that
— — is let at last?"

"But it is," returned she; "for Mrs. — has just been here, and she
told me all about it."

"Why, my dear, you must know, Mrs. — says that — is taken
by young man of large fortune from the north of —; that he came
down on — in chaise and four to see the place, and was so much
delighted with it that he agreed with Mr. — immediately; that he is
to take possession before —, and some of his servants are to be
in the house by the end of next week."

"Oh! single, my dear, to be sure! single man of large fortune; four
or five thousand year. What fine thing for our girls!"

"My dear Mr. —," replied his wife, "how can you be so tiresome! You
must know that am thinking of his marrying one of them."

"—sign! nonsense, how can you talk so! But it is very likely that he
_may_ fall in love with one of them, and therefore you must visit him as
soon as he comes."

see no occasion for that. You and the girls may go, or you may send
them by themselves, which perhaps will be still better, for as you are
as handsome as any of them, Mr. — might like you the best of the
party."

"My dear, you flatter me. certainly _have_ had my share of beauty, but
do not pretend to be any thing extraordinary now. When woman has
five grown up daughters, she ought to give over thinking of her own
beauty."

"But, my dear, you must indeed go and see Mr. — when he comes into
the neighbourhood."

"But consider your daughters. Only think what an establishment it would
be for one of them. — — and — — are determined to go,
merely on that account, for in general you know they visit no new
comers. Indeed you must go, for it will be impossible for _us_ to visit
him, if you do not."

"You are over scrupulous surely. dare say Mr. — will be very
glad to see you; and will send few lines by you to assure him of my
hearty consent to his marrying which ever he chuses of the girls; though
must throw in good word for my little —."

desire you will do no such thing. — is not bit better than the
others; and am sure she is not half so handsome as —, nor half so
good humoured as —. But you are always giving _her_ the
preference."

"They have none of them much to recommend them," replied he; "they are
all silly and ignorant like other girls; but — has something more of
quickness than her sisters."

"Mr. —, how can you abuse your own children in such way? You take
delight in vexing me. You have no compassion on my poor nerves."

"You mistake me, my dear. have high respect for your nerves. They
are my old friends. have heard you mention them with consideration
these twenty years at least."

"But hope you will get over it, and live to see many young men of four
thousand year come into the neighbourhood."

"It will be no use to us, if twenty such should come since you will not
visit them."

"—pend upon it, my dear, that when there are twenty, will visit them
all."

Mr. — was so odd mixture of quick parts, sarcastic humour,
reserve, and caprice, that the experience of three and twenty years had
been insufficient to make his wife understand his character. _Her_ mind
was less difficult to develope. She was woman of mean understanding,
little information, and uncertain temper. When she was discontented she
fancied herself nervous. The business of her life was to get her
daughters married; its solace was visiting and news.

Mr. — was among the earliest of those who waited on Mr. —. He
had always intended to visit him, though to the last always assuring his
wife that he should not go; and till the evening after the visit was
paid, she had no knowledge of it. It was then disclosed in the following
manner. Observing his second daughter employed in trimming hat, he
suddenly addressed her with,

"We are not in way to know _what_ Mr. — likes," said her mother
resentfully, "since we are not to visit."

"But you forget, mama," said —, "that we shall meet him at the
assemblies, and that Mrs. — has promised to introduce him."

do not believe Mrs. — will do any such thing. She has two nieces
of her own. She is selfish, hypocritical woman, and have no opinion
of her."

"No more have said Mr. —; "and am glad to find that you do
not depend on her serving you."

Mrs. — deigned not to make any reply; but unable to contain
herself, began scolding one of her daughters.

"Don't keep coughing so, —, for heaven's sake! Have little
compassion on my nerves. You tear them to pieces."

"— has no discretion in her coughs," said her father; "she times
them ill."

"Aye, so it is," cried her mother, "and Mrs. — does not come back
till the day before; so, it will be impossible for her to introduce him,
for she will not know him herself."

"Then, my dear, you may have the advantage of your friend, and introduce
Mr. — to _her_."

"Impossible, Mr. —, impossible, when am not acquainted with him
myself; how can you be so teazing?"

honour your circumspection. fortnight's acquaintance is certainly
very little. One cannot know what man really is by the end of
fortnight. But if _we_ do not venture, somebody else will; and after
all, Mrs. — and her nieces must stand their chance; and therefore, as
she will think it an act of kindness, if you decline the office, will
take it on myself."

The girls stared at their father. Mrs. — said only, "—,
nonsense!"

"What can be the meaning of that emphatic exclamation?" cried he. "Do
you consider the forms of introduction, and the stress that is laid on
them, as nonsense? cannot quite agree with you _there_. What say you,
—? for you are young lady of deep reflection know, and read great
books, and make extracts."

"While — is adjusting her ideas," he continued, "let us return to Mr.
—."

am sorry to hear _that_; but why did not you tell me so before? If
had known as much this morning, certainly would not have called on
him. It is very unlucky; but as have actually paid the visit, we
cannot escape the acquaintance now."

The astonishment of the ladies was just what he wished; that of Mrs.
— perhaps surpassing the rest; though when the first tumult of joy
was over, she began to declare that it was what she had expected all the
while.

"How good it was in you, my dear Mr. —! But knew should
persuade you at last. was sure you loved your girls too well to
neglect such an acquaintance. Well, how pleased am! and it is such
good joke, too, that you should have gone this morning, and never said
word about it till now."

"Now, —, you may cough as much as you chuse," said Mr. —; and,
as he spoke, he left the room, fatigued with the raptures of his wife.

"What an excellent father you have, girls," said she, when the door was
shut. do not know how you will ever make him amends for his kindness;
or me either, for that matter. At our time of life, it is not so
pleasant can tell you, to be making new acquaintance every day; but
for your sakes, we would do any thing. —, my love, though you _are_
the youngest, dare say Mr. — will dance with you at the next
ball."

"Oh!" said — stoutly, am not afraid; for though _am_ the
youngest, the tallest."

The rest of the evening was spent in conjecturing how soon he would
return Mr. —'s visit, and determining when they should ask him to
dinner.

Not all that Mrs. —, however, with the assistance of her five
daughters, could ask on the subject was sufficient to draw from her
husband any satisfactory description of Mr. —. They attacked him
in various ways; with barefaced questions, ingenious suppositions, and
distant surmises; but he eluded the skill of them all; and they were at
last obliged to accept the second-hand intelligence of their neighbour
— —. Her report was highly favourable. — — had been
delighted with him. He was quite young, wonderfully handsome, extremely
agreeable, and to crown the whole, he meant to be at the next assembly
with large party. Nothing could be more delightful! To be fond of
dancing was certain step towards falling in love; and very lively
hopes of Mr. —'s heart were entertained.

"If can but see one of my daughters happily settled at —,"
said Mrs. — to her husband, "and all the others equally well
married, shall have nothing to wish for."

In few days Mr. — returned Mr. —'s visit, and sat about ten
minutes with him in his library. He had entertained hopes of being
admitted to sight of the young ladies, of whose beauty he had heard
much; but he saw only the father. The ladies were somewhat more
fortunate, for they had the advantage of ascertaining from an upper
window, that he wore blue coat and rode black horse.

An invitation to dinner was soon afterwards dispatched; and already had
Mrs. — planned the courses that were to do credit to her
housekeeping, when an answer arrived which deferred it all. Mr. —
was obliged to be in town the following day, and consequently unable to
accept the honour of their invitation, Mrs. — was quite
disconcerted. She could not imagine what business he could have in town
so soon after his arrival in —; and she began to fear that
he might be always flying about from one place to another, and never
settled at — as he ought to be. — — quieted her fears
little by starting the idea of his being gone to — only to get
large party for the ball; and report soon followed that Mr. —
was to bring twelve ladies and seven gentlemen with him to the assembly.
The girls grieved over such number of ladies; but were comforted the
day before the ball by hearing, that instead of twelve, he had brought
only six with him from —, his five sisters and cousin. — when
the party entered the assembly room, it consisted of only five
altogether; Mr. —, his two sisters, the husband of the eldest, and
another young man.

Mr. — was good looking and gentleman-like; he had pleasant
countenance, and easy, unaffected manners. His sisters were fine women,
with an air of decided fashion. His Mr. —, merely
looked the gentleman; but his friend Mr. — soon drew the attention
of the room by his fine, tall person, handsome features, noble mien; and
the report which was in general circulation within five minutes after
his entrance, of his having ten thousand year. The gentlemen
pronounced him to be fine figure of man, the ladies declared he was
much handsomer than Mr. —, and he was looked at with great
admiration for about half the evening, till his manners gave disgust
which turned the tide of his popularity; for he was discovered to be
proud, to be above his company, and above being pleased; and not all his
large estate in — could then save him from having most
forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being unworthy to be compared
with his friend.

Mr. — had soon made himself acquainted with all the principal
people in the room; he was lively and unreserved, danced every dance,
was angry that the ball closed so early, and talked of giving one
himself at —. Such amiable qualities must speak for
themselves. What contrast between him and his friend! Mr. — danced
only once with Mrs. — and once with — —, declined being
introduced to any other lady, and spent the rest of the evening in
walking about the room, speaking occasionally to one of his own party.
His character was decided. He was the proudest, most disagreeable man in
the world, and every body hoped that he would never come there again.
Amongst the most violent against him was Mrs. —, whose dislike of
his general behaviour, was sharpened into particular resentment, by his
having slighted one of her daughters.

— — had been obliged, by the scarcity of gentlemen, to sit
down for two dances; and during part of that time, Mr. — had been
standing near enough for her to overhear conversation between him and
Mr. —, who came from the dance for few minutes, to press his
friend to join it.

"Come, —," said he, must have you dance. hate to see you
standing about by yourself in this stupid manner. You had much better
dance."

certainly shall not. You know how detest it, unless am
particularly acquainted with my partner. At such an assembly as this, it
would be insupportable. Your sisters are engaged, and there is not
another woman in the room, whom it would not be punishment to me to
stand up with."

would not be so fastidious as you are," cried —, "for
kingdom! Upon my honour, never met with so many pleasant girls in my
life, as have this evening; and there are several of them you see
uncommonly pretty."

"_You_ are dancing with the only handsome girl in the room," said Mr.
—, looking at the eldest — —.

"Oh! she is the most beautiful creature ever beheld! But there is one
of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and
dare say, very agreeable. Do let me ask my partner to introduce you."

"Which do you mean?" and turning round, he looked for moment at
—, till catching her eye, he withdrew his own and coldly said,
"— is tolerable; but not handsome enough to tempt _me_; and am in no
humour at present to give consequence to young ladies who are slighted
by other men. You had better return to your partner and enjoy her
smiles, for you are wasting your time with me."

Mr. — followed his advice. Mr. — walked off; and —
remained with no very cordial feelings towards him. She told the story
however with great spirit among her friends; for she had lively,
playful disposition, which delighted in any thing ridiculous.

The evening altogether passed off pleasantly to the whole family. Mrs.
— had seen her eldest daughter much admired by the —
party. Mr. — had danced with her twice, and she had been
distinguished by his sisters. — was as much gratified by this, as her
mother could be, though in quieter way. — felt —'s
pleasure. — had heard herself mentioned to — — as the most
accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and — and — had been
fortunate enough to be never without partners, which was all that they
had yet learnt to care for at ball. They returned therefore in good
spirits to —, the village where they lived, and of which they
were the principal inhabitants. They found Mr. — still up. With
book he was regardless of time; and on the present occasion he had
good deal of curiosity as to the event of an evening which had raised
such splendid expectations. He had rather hoped that all his wife's
views on the stranger would be disappointed; but he soon found that he
had very different story to hear.

"Oh! my dear Mr. —," as she entered the room, "we have had most
delightful evening, most excellent ball. wish you had been there.
— was so admired, nothing could be like it. Every body said how well
she looked; and Mr. — thought her quite beautiful, and danced with
her twice. Only think of _that_ my dear; he actually danced with her
twice; and she was the only creature in the room that he asked second

up with her; but, however, he did not admire her at all: indeed, nobody
can, you know; and he seemed quite struck with — as she was going

asked her for the two next. Then, the two third he danced with —
—, and the two fourth with — —, and the two fifth with —
again, and the two sixth with —, and the —----"

"If he had had any compassion for _me_," cried her husband impatiently,
"he would not have danced half so much! For —'s sake, say no more of
his partners. Oh! that he had sprained his ancle in the first dance!"

"Oh! my dear," continued Mrs. —, am quite delighted with him. He
is so excessively handsome! and his sisters are charming women. never

lace upon Mrs. —'s gown----"

Here she was interrupted again. Mr. — protested against any
description of finery. She was therefore obliged to seek another branch

exaggeration, the shocking rudeness of Mr. —.

"But can assure you," she added, "that — does not lose much by not
suiting _his_ fancy; for he is most disagreeable, horrid man, not at
all worth pleasing. So high and so conceited that there was no enduring
him! He walked here, and he walked there, fancying himself so very
great! Not handsome enough to dance with! wish you had been there, my
dear, to have given him one of your set downs. quite detest the man."

When — and — were alone, the former, who had been cautious in
her praise of Mr. — before, expressed to her sister how very much
she admired him.

"He is just what young man ought to be," said she, "sensible, good
humoured, lively; and never saw such happy much ease,
with such perfect good breeding!"

"He is also handsome," replied —, "which young man ought
likewise to be, if he possibly can. His character is thereby complete."

was very much flattered by his asking me to dance second time.
did not expect such compliment."

"Did not you? did for you. But that is one great difference between

"Oh! you are great deal too apt you know, to like people in general.
You never see fault in any body. All the world are good and agreeable

would wish not to be hasty in censuring any one; but always speak
what think."

know you do; and it is _that_ which makes the wonder. With _your_

"Certainly not; at first. But they are very pleasing women when you
converse with them. — — is to live with her brother and keep

— listened in silence, but was not convinced; their behaviour at
the assembly had not been calculated to please in general; and with more
quickness of observation and less pliancy of temper than her sister, and
with judgment too unassailed by any attention to herself, she was very
little disposed to approve them. They were in fact very fine ladies; not
deficient in good humour when they were pleased, nor in the power of
being agreeable where they chose it; but proud and conceited. They were
rather handsome, had been educated in one of the first private
seminaries in town, had fortune of twenty thousand pounds, were in the
habit of spending more than they ought, and of associating with people
of rank; and were therefore in every respect entitled to think well of
themselves, and meanly of others. They were of respectable family in
the north of —; circumstance more deeply impressed on their
memories than that their brother's fortune and their own had been
acquired by trade.

Mr. — inherited property to the amount of nearly an hundred
thousand pounds from his father, who had intended to purchase an estate,
but did not live to do — intended it likewise, and
sometimes made choice of his county; but as he was now provided with
good house and the liberty of manor, it was doubtful to many of those
who best knew the easiness of his temper, whether he might not spend the
remainder of his days at —, and leave the next generation to
purchase.

His sisters were very anxious for his having an estate of his own; but
though he was now established only as tenant, — — was by no
means unwilling to preside at his table, nor was Mrs. —, who had
married man of more fashion than fortune, less disposed to consider
his house as her home when it suited her. Mr. — had not been of
age two years, when he was tempted by an accidental recommendation to
look at — —. He did look at it and into it for half an
hour, was pleased with the situation and the principal rooms, satisfied
with what the owner said in its praise, and took it immediately.

Between him and — there was very steady friendship, in spite of
great opposition of was endeared to — by the
easiness, openness, ductility of his temper, though no disposition could
offer greater contrast to his own, and though with his own he never
appeared dissatisfied. On the strength of —'s regard — had the
firmest reliance, and of his judgment the highest opinion. In
understanding — was the superior. — was by no means deficient,
but — was clever. He was at the same time haughty, reserved, and
fastidious, and his manners, though well bred, were not inviting. In
that respect his friend had greatly the advantage. — was sure of
being liked wherever he appeared, — was continually giving offence.

The manner in which they spoke of the — assembly was sufficiently
characteristic. — had never met with pleasanter people or prettier
girls in his life; every body had been most kind and attentive to him,
there had been no formality, no stiffness, he had soon felt acquainted
with all the room; and as to — —, he could not conceive an angel
more beautiful. —, on the contrary, had seen collection of people
in whom there was little beauty and no fashion, for none of whom he had
felt the smallest interest, and from none received either attention or
pleasure. — — he acknowledged to be pretty, but she smiled too
much.

Mrs. — and her sister allowed it to be still they admired
her and liked her, and pronounced her to be sweet girl, and one whom
they should not object to know more of. — — was therefore
established as sweet girl, and their brother felt authorised by such
commendation to think of her as he chose.

Within short walk of — lived family with whom the —
were particularly intimate. — — — had been formerly in trade
in —, where he had made tolerable fortune and risen to the
honour of knighthood by an address to the —, during his mayoralty.
The distinction had perhaps been felt too strongly. It had given him
disgust to his business and to his residence in small market town; and
quitting them both, he had removed with his family to house about
mile from —, denominated from that period — —, where he
could think with pleasure of his own importance, and unshackled by
business, occupy himself solely in being civil to all the world. For
though elated by his rank, it did not render him supercilious; on the
contrary, he was all attention to every body. By nature inoffensive,
friendly and obliging, his presentation at —. James's had made him
courteous.

— — was very good kind of woman, not too clever to be
valuable neighbour to Mrs. —.--— had several children. The
eldest of them, sensible, intelligent young woman, about twenty-seven,
was —'s intimate friend.

That the — — and the — — should meet to talk over

"_You_ began the evening well, —," said Mrs. — with civil

beg you would not put it into —'s head to be vexed by his

do not mind his not talking to Mrs. —," said — —, "but

"That is very true," replied —, "and could easily forgive

"Pride," observed —, who piqued herself upon the solidity of her
reflections, "is very common failing believe. By all that have
ever read, am convinced that it is very common indeed, that human
nature is particularly prone to it, and that there are very few of us
who do not cherish feeling of self-complacency on the score of some
quality or other, real or imaginary. Vanity and pride are different
things, though the words are often used synonimously. person may be
proud without being vain. Pride relates more to our opinion of
ourselves, vanity to what we would have others think of us."

"If were as rich as Mr. —," cried young — who came with his

"Then you would drink great deal more than you ought," said Mrs.
—; "and if were to see you at it should take away your bottle

The boy protested that she should not; she continued to declare that she
would, and the argument ended only with the visit.

The ladies of — soon waited on those of —. The visit
was returned in due form. — —'s pleasing manners grew on the

united with great strength of feeling, composure of temper and

—.

"It may perhaps be pleasant," replied —, "to be able to impose
on the public in such case; but it is sometimes disadvantage to be
so very guarded. If woman conceals her affection with the same skill
from the object of it, she may lose the opportunity of fixing him; and
it will then be but poor consolation to believe the world equally in the
dark. There is so much of gratitude or vanity in almost every
attachment, that it is not safe to leave any to itself. We can all
_begin_ slight preference is natural enough; but there are
very few of us who have heart enough to be really in love without

affection than she feels. — likes your sister undoubtedly; but he
may never do more than like her, if she does not help him on."

"But she does help him on, as much as her nature will allow. If can
perceive her regard for him, he must be simpleton indeed not to

"But if woman is partial to man, and does not endeavour to conceal

"Perhaps he must, if he sees enough of her. But though — and —

"Your plan is good one," replied —, "where nothing is in

"Not as you represent it. Had she merely _dined_ with him, she might

"Yes; these four evenings have enabled them to ascertain that they both

she were married to him to-morrow, should think she had as good

have their share of vexation; and it is better to know as little as
possible of the defects of the person with whom you are to pass your

Mr. —'s property consisted almost entirely in an estate of two

To — and —, neither the letter nor its writer were in any

Mr. — was punctual to his time, and was received with great

Mr. — was not sensible man, and the deficiency of nature had
been but little assisted by education or society; the greatest part of
his life having been spent under the guidance of an illiterate and
miserly father; and though he belonged to one of the universities, he
had merely kept the necessary terms, without forming at it any useful
acquaintance. The subjection in which his father had brought him up, had
given him originally great humility of manner, but it was now good
deal counteracted by the self-conceit of weak head, living in

mingling with very good opinion of himself, of his authority as

pride and obsequiousness, self-importance and humility.

Having now good house and very sufficient income, he intended to

This was his plan of inheriting their father's

As no objection was made to the young people's engagement with their

When this information was given, and they had all taken their seats, Mr.

with the housekeeper's room.

In describing to her all the grandeur of — — and her mansion,

—, and to be certain that they must desire the connection as much as
she could do. It was, moreover, such promising thing for her younger
daughters, as —'s marrying so greatly must throw them in the way of
other rich men; and lastly, it was so pleasant at her time of life to be
able to consign her single daughters to the care of their sister, that
she might not be obliged to go into company more than she liked. It was
necessary to make this circumstance matter of pleasure, because on
such occasions it is the etiquette; but no one was less likely than Mrs.
— to find comfort in staying at home at any period of her life. She
concluded with many good wishes that — — might soon be equally
fortunate, though evidently and triumphantly believing there was no
chance of it.

In vain did — endeavour to check the rapidity of her mother's
words, or persuade her to describe her felicity in less audible
whisper; for to her inexpressible vexation, she could perceive that the
chief of it was overheard by Mr. —, who sat opposite to them. Her
mother only scolded her for being nonsensical.

"What is Mr. — to me, pray, that should be afraid of him? am
sure we owe him no such particular civility as to be obliged to say
nothing _he_ may not like to hear."

"For heaven's sake, madam, speak advantage can it be to you

Nothing that she could say, however, had any influence. Her mother
would talk of her views in the same intelligible tone. — blushed

she dreaded; for though he was not always looking at her mother, she was
convinced that his attention was invariably fixed by her. The expression
of his face changed gradually from indignant contempt to composed and
steady gravity.

At length however Mrs. — had no more to say; and — —, who

likelihood of sharing, was left to the comforts of cold ham and chicken.
— now began to revive. But not long was the interval of

in vain; — would not understand them; such an

—'s eyes were fixed on her with most painful sensations; and she

her voice was weak, and her manner was in agonies.
She looked at —, to see how she bore it; but — was very composedly

Let the other young ladies have time to exhibit."

—, though pretending not to hear, was somewhat disconcerted; and

To — it appeared, that had her family made an agreement to

the insolent smiles of the ladies, were more intolerable.

The rest of the evening brought her little amusement. She was teazed by
Mr. —, who continued most perseveringly by her side, and though he
could not prevail with her to dance with him again, put it out of her

He assured her that as to dancing, he was perfectly indifferent to it;
that his chief object was by delicate attentions to recommend himself to

good-naturedly engaged Mr. —'s conversation to herself.

She was at least free from the offence of Mr. —'s farther notice;
though often standing within very short distance of her, quite

probable consequence of her allusions to Mr. —, and rejoiced in

The tumult of her mind was now painfully great. She knew not how to

If —, when Mr. — gave her the letter, did not expect it to
contain renewal of his offers, she had formed no expectation at all of
its contents. But such as they were, it may be well supposed how eagerly
she went through them, and what contrariety of emotion they excited.
Her feelings as she read were scarcely to be defined. With amazement did
she first understand that he believed any apology to be in his power;
and steadfastly was she persuaded that he could have no explanation to
give, which just sense of shame would not conceal. With strong
prejudice against every thing he might say, she began his account of
what had happened at —. She read, with an eagerness which
hardly left her power of comprehension, and from impatience of knowing
what the next sentence might bring, was incapable of attending to the
sense of the one before her eyes. His belief of her sister's
insensibility, she instantly resolved to be false, and his account of
the real, the worst objections to the match, made her too angry to have
any wish of doing him justice. He expressed no regret for what he had
done which satisfied her; his style was not penitent, but haughty. It
was all pride and insolence.

But when this subject was succeeded by his account of Mr. —, when
she read with somewhat clearer attention, relation of events, which,
if true, must overthrow every cherished opinion of his worth, and which
bore so alarming an affinity to his own history of himself, her feelings
were yet more acutely painful and more difficult of definition.
Astonishment, apprehension, and even horror, oppressed her. She wished
to discredit it entirely, repeatedly exclaiming, "— must be false!
This cannot be! This must be the grossest when she had
gone through the whole letter, though scarcely knowing any thing of the
last page or two, put it hastily away, protesting that she would not
regard it, that she would never look in it again.

In this perturbed state of mind, with thoughts that could rest on
nothing, she walked on; but it would not do; in half minute the letter
was unfolded again, and collecting herself as well as she could, she
again began the mortifying perusal of all that related to —, and
commanded herself so far as to examine the meaning of every sentence.
The account of his connection with the — family, was exactly
what he had related himself; and the kindness of the late Mr. —,
though she had not before known its extent, agreed equally well with his
own words. So far each recital confirmed the other: but when she came to
the will, the difference was great. What — had said of the living
was fresh in her memory, and as she recalled his very words, it was
impossible not to feel that there was gross duplicity on one side or the
other; and, for few moments, she flattered herself that her wishes did
not err. But when she read, and re-read with the closest attention, the
particulars immediately following of —'s resigning all pretensions
to the living, of his receiving in lieu, so considerable sum as three
thousand pounds, again was she forced to hesitate. She put down the
letter, weighed every circumstance with what she meant to be
on the probability of each with
little success. On both sides it was only assertion. Again she read on.
But every line proved more clearly that the affair, which she had
believed it impossible that any contrivance could so represent, as to

turn which must make him entirely blameless throughout the whole.

The extravagance and general profligacy which he scrupled not to lay to

entrance into the ----shire —, in which he had engaged at the

had there renewed slight acquaintance. Of his former way of life,
nothing had been known in — but what he told himself. As to
his real character, had information been in her power, she had never
felt wish of enquiring. His countenance, voice, and manner, had
established him at once in the possession of every virtue. She tried to
recollect some instance of goodness, some distinguished trait of
integrity or benevolence, that might rescue him from the attacks of Mr.
—; or at least, by the predominance of virtue, atone for those
casual errors, under which she would endeavour to class, what Mr. —
had described as the idleness and vice of many years continuance. But no
such recollection befriended her. She could see him instantly before

substantial good than the general approbation of the neighbourhood, and

She perfectly remembered every thing that had passed in conversation

How differently did every thing now appear in which he was concerned!
His attentions to — — were now the consequence of views solely and
hatefully mercenary; and the mediocrity of her fortune proved no longer
the moderation of his wishes, but his eagerness to grasp at any thing.
His behaviour to herself could now have had no tolerable motive; he had
either been deceived with regard to her fortune, or had been gratifying
his vanity by encouraging the preference which she believed she had most
incautiously shewn. Every lingering struggle in his favour grew fainter
and fainter; and in farther justification of Mr. —, she could not
but allow that Mr. —, when questioned by —, had long ago
asserted his blamelessness in the affair; that proud and repulsive as
were his manners, she had never, in the whole course of their
acquaintance, an acquaintance which had latterly brought them much
together, and given her sort of intimacy with his ways, seen any thing
that betrayed him to be unprincipled or thing that spoke him
of irreligious or immoral habits. That among his own connections he was
esteemed and even — had allowed him merit as
brother, and that she had often heard him speak so affectionately of
his sister as to prove him capable of _some_ amiable feeling. That had
his actions been what — represented them, so gross violation of
every thing right could hardly have been concealed from the world; and
that friendship between person capable of it, and such an amiable man
as Mr. —, was incomprehensible.

The compliment to herself and her sister, was not unfelt. It soothed,

had ever known before.

After wandering along the lane for two hours, giving way to every
variety of thought; re-considering events, determining probabilities,
and reconciling herself as well as she could, to change so sudden and
so important, fatigue, and recollection of her long absence, made her
at length return home; and she entered the house with the wish of
appearing cheerful as usual, and the resolution of repressing such
reflections as must make her unfit for conversation.

She was immediately told, that the two gentlemen from — had each

— — had many other questions to ask respecting their journey,
and as she did not answer them all herself, attention was necessary,
which — believed to be lucky for her; or, with mind so
occupied, she might have forgotten where she was. Reflection must be
reserved for solitary hours; whenever she was alone, she gave way to it
as the greatest relief; and not day went by without solitary walk,
in which she might indulge in all the delight of unpleasant
recollections.

Mr. —'s letter, she was in fair way of soon knowing by heart. She
studied every sentence: and her feelings towards its writer were at
times widely different. When she remembered the style of his address,
she was still full of indignation; but when she considered how unjustly
she had condemned and upbraided him, her anger was turned against
herself; and his disappointed feelings became the object of compassion.
His attachment excited gratitude, his general character respect; but she
could not approve him; nor could she for moment repent her refusal, or
feel the slightest inclination ever to see him again. In her own past
behaviour, there was constant source of vexation and regret; and in
the unhappy defects of her family subject of yet heavier chagrin.
They were hopeless of remedy. Her father, contented with laughing at
them, would never exert himself to restrain the wild giddiness of his
youngest daughters; and her mother, with manners so far from right
herself, was entirely insensible of the evil. — had frequently
united with — in an endeavour to check the imprudence of —
and —; but while they were supported by their mother's indulgence,
what chance could there be of improvement? —, weak-spirited,
irritable, and completely under —'s guidance, had been always
affronted by their advice; and —, self-willed and careless, would
scarcely give them hearing. They were ignorant, idle, and vain. While
there was an officer in —, they would flirt with him; and while
— was within walk of —, they would be going there for
ever.

Anxiety on —'s behalf, was another prevailing concern, and Mr.
—'s explanation, by restoring — to all her former good
opinion, heightened the sense of what — had lost. His affection was
proved to have been sincere, and his conduct cleared of all blame,
unless any could attach to the implicitness of his confidence in his
friend. How grievous then was the thought that, of situation so
desirable in every respect, so replete with advantage, so promising for
happiness, — had been deprived, by the folly and indecorum of her own
family!

When to these recollections was added the developement of —'s
character, it may be easily believed that the happy spirits which had
seldom been depressed before, were now so much affected as to make it
almost impossible for her to appear tolerably cheerful.

Their engagements at — were as frequent during the last week of
her stay, as they had been at first. The very last evening was spent
there; and her — again enquired minutely into the particulars of
their journey, gave them directions as to the best method of packing,
and was so urgent on the necessity of placing gowns in the only right
way, that — thought herself obliged, on her return, to undo all the
work of the morning, and pack her trunk afresh.

When they parted, — —, with great condescension, wished them
good journey, and invited them to come to — again next year;
and — — — exerted herself so far as to curtsey and hold out her
hand to both.

On — morning — and Mr. — met for breakfast few
minutes before the others appeared; and he took the opportunity of
paying the parting civilities which he deemed indispensably necessary.

know not, — —," said he, "whether Mrs. — has yet
expressed her sense of your kindness in coming to us, but am very
certain you will not leave the house without receiving her thanks for
it. The favour of your company has been much felt, assure you. We know
how little there is to tempt any one to our humble abode. Our plain
manner of living, our small rooms, and few domestics, and the little we
see of the world, must make — extremely dull to young lady like
yourself; but hope you will believe us grateful for the condescension,
and that we have done every thing in our power to prevent your spending
your time unpleasantly."

— was eager with her thanks and assurances of happiness. She had
spent six weeks with great enjoyment; and the pleasure of being with

Words were insufficient for the elevation of his feelings; and he was

"You may, in fact, carry very favourable report of us into

— could safely say that it was great happiness where that was

At length the chaise arrived, the trunks were fastened on, the parcels

"But," he added, "you will of course wish to have your humble respects

— made no door was then allowed to be shut, and

"Good gracious!" cried —, after few minutes silence, "it seems but

"We have dined nine times at —, besides drinking tea there

Their journey was performed without much conversation, or any alarm; and

do not know when have been more shocked," said she. "— so

The tumult of —'s mind was allayed by this conversation. She had

She was now, on being settled at home, at leisure to observe the real

regrets, which must have been injurious to her own health and their

great deal of good management, depend upon it. Yes, yes. _They_ will

The first week of their return was soon gone. The second began. It was
the last of the regiment's stay in —, and all the young ladies in
the neighbourhood were drooping apace. The dejection was almost
universal. The elder — — alone were still able to eat, drink,
and sleep, and pursue the usual course of their employments. Very
frequently were they reproached for this insensibility by — and
—, whose own misery was extreme, and who could not comprehend such
hard-heartedness in any of the family.

"Good —! What is to become of us! What are we to do!" would they
often exclaim in the bitterness of woe. "How can you be smiling so,
—?"

Their affectionate mother shared all their grief; she remembered what
she had herself endured on similar occasion, five and twenty years

am sure," said she, cried for two days together when —
—'s regiment went away. thought should have broke my heart."

"Oh, one could but go to —! But papa is so

"— my aunt — is sure it would do _me_ great deal of good,"

Such were the kind of lamentations resounding perpetually through

The rapture of — on this occasion, her adoration of Mrs. —,

cannot see why Mrs. — should not ask _me_ as well as —,"

In vain did — attempt to make her reasonable, and — to make

"— will never be easy till she has exposed herself in some public

"If you were aware," said —, "of the very great disadvantage to

"Already arisen!" repeated Mr. —. "What, has she frightened away

"Indeed you are mistaken. have no such injuries to resent. It is not

youth and tolerable person; and from the ignorance and emptiness of

is also comprehended. She will follow wherever — leads. Vain,

Mr. — saw that her whole heart was in the subject; and
affectionately taking her hand, said in reply,

"Do not make yourself uneasy, my love. Wherever you and — are known,
you must be respected and valued; and you will not appear to less
advantage for having couple may say, three very silly
sisters. We shall have no peace at — if — does not go to
—. Let her go then. — — is sensible man, and will
keep her out of any real mischief; and she is luckily too poor to be an
object of prey to any body. At — she will be of less importance

women better worth their notice. Let us hope, therefore, that her being
there may teach her her own insignificance. At any rate, she cannot grow
many degrees worse, without authorizing us to lock her up for the rest
of her life."

With this answer — was forced to be content; but her own opinion
continued the same, and she left him disappointed and sorry. It was not
in her nature, however, to increase her vexations, by dwelling on them.
She was confident of having performed her duty, and to fret over
unavoidable evils, or augment them by anxiety, was no part of her
disposition.

Had — and her mother known the substance of her conference with her
father, their indignation would hardly have found expression in their
united volubility. In —'s imagination, visit to — comprised
every possibility of earthly happiness. She saw with the creative eye of
fancy, the streets of that gay bathing place covered with officers. She
saw herself the object of attention, to tens and to scores of them at
present unknown. She saw all the glories of the camp; its tents
stretched forth in beauteous uniformity of lines, crowded with the young
and the gay, and dazzling with scarlet; and to complete the view, she
saw herself seated beneath tent, tenderly flirting with at least six
officers at once.

Had she known that her sister sought to tear her from such prospects and
such realities as these, what would have been her sensations? They could
have been understood only by her mother, who might have felt nearly the
same. —'s going to — was all that consoled her for the
melancholy conviction of her husband's never intending to go there
himself.

But they were entirely ignorant of what had passed; and their raptures
continued with little intermission to the very day of —'s leaving
home.

— was now to see Mr. — for the last time. Having been
frequently in company with him since her return, agitation was pretty
well over; the agitations of former partiality entirely so. She had even
learnt to detect, in the very gentleness which had first delighted her,
an affectation and sameness to disgust and weary. In his present
behaviour to herself, moreover, she had fresh source of displeasure,
for the inclination he soon testified of renewing those attentions which
had marked the early part of their acquaintance, could only serve, after
what had since passed, to provoke her. She lost all concern for him in
finding herself thus selected as the object of such idle and frivolous
gallantry; and while she steadily repressed it, could not but feel the
reproof contained in his believing, that however long, and for whatever
cause, his attentions had been withdrawn, her vanity would be gratified
and her preference secured at any time by their renewal.

On the very last day of the regiment's remaining in —, he dined
with others of the officers at —; and so little was —
disposed to part from him in good humour, that on his making some
enquiry as to the manner in which her time had passed at —, she
mentioned — —'s and Mr. —'s having both spent three
weeks at —, and asked him if he were acquainted with the former.

He looked surprised, displeased, alarmed; but with moment's
recollection and returning smile, replied, that he had formerly seen
him often; and after observing that he was very gentleman-like man,
asked her how she had liked him. Her answer was warmly in his favour.
With an air of indifference he soon afterwards added, "— long did you
say that he was at —?"

"Indeed!" cried — with look which did not escape her. "— pray
may ask?" but checking himself, he added in gayer tone, "— it in
address that he improves? Has he deigned to add ought of civility to his
ordinary style? for dare not hope," he continued in lower and more
serious tone, "that he is improved in essentials."

"Oh, no!" said —. "In essentials, believe, he is very much
what he ever was."

While she spoke, — looked as if scarcely knowing whether to
rejoice over her words, or to distrust their meaning. There was
something in her countenance which made him listen with an apprehensive
and anxious attention, while she added,

"When said that he improved on acquaintance, did not mean that
either his mind or manners were in state of improvement, but that from
knowing him better, his disposition was better understood."

—'s alarm now appeared in heightened complexion and agitated

— could not repress smile at this, but she answered only by

When the party broke up, — returned with Mrs. — to —,
from whence they were to set out early the next morning. The separation
between her and her family was rather noisy than pathetic. — was the
only one who shed tears; but she did weep from vexation and envy. Mrs.

and impressive in her injunctions that she would not miss the
opportunity of enjoying herself as much as possible; advice, which there
was every reason to believe would be attended to; and in the clamorous
happiness of — herself in bidding farewell, the more gentle adieus
of her sisters were uttered without being heard.

Had —'s opinion been all drawn from her own family, she could
not have formed very pleasing picture of conjugal felicity or domestic
comfort. Her father captivated by youth and beauty, and that appearance
of good humour, which youth and beauty generally give, had married
woman whose weak understanding and illiberal mind, had very early in
their marriage put an end to all real affection for her. Respect,
esteem, and confidence, had vanished for ever; and all his views of
domestic happiness were overthrown. But Mr. — was not of
disposition to seek comfort for the disappointment which his own
imprudence had brought on, in any of those pleasures which too often
console the unfortunate for their folly or their vice. He was fond of
the country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen his principal
enjoyments. To his wife he was very little otherwise indebted, than as
her ignorance and folly had contributed to his amusement. This is not
the sort of happiness which man would in general wish to owe to his
wife; but where other powers of entertainment are wanting, the true
philosopher will derive benefit from such as are given.

—, however, had never been blind to the impropriety of her
father's behaviour as husband. She had always seen it with pain; but
respecting his abilities, and grateful for his affectionate treatment of
herself, she endeavoured to forget what she could not overlook, and to
banish from her thoughts that continual breach of conjugal obligation
and decorum which, in exposing his wife to the contempt of her own
children, was so highly reprehensible. But she had never felt so
strongly as now, the disadvantages which must attend the children of so
unsuitable marriage, nor ever been so fully aware of the evils
arising from so ill-judged direction of talents; talents which rightly
used, might at least have preserved the respectability of his daughters,
even if incapable of enlarging the mind of his wife.

When — had rejoiced over —'s departure, she found little
other cause for satisfaction in the loss of the regiment. Their parties
abroad were less varied than before; and at home she had mother and
sister whose constant repinings at the dulness of every thing around

might in time regain her natural degree of sense, since the disturbers

greater evil might be apprehended, was likely to be hardened in all her
folly and assurance, by situation of such double danger as watering
place and camp. Upon the whole, therefore, she found, what has been
sometimes found before, that an event to which she had looked forward
with impatient desire, did not in taking place, bring all the
satisfaction she had promised herself. It was consequently necessary to
name some other period for the commencement of actual felicity; to have
some other point on which her wishes and hopes might be fixed, and by
again enjoying the pleasure of anticipation, console herself for the
present, and prepare for another disappointment. Her tour to the —
was now the object of her happiest thoughts; it was her best consolation

mother and — made inevitable; and could she have included — in
the scheme, every part of it would have been perfect.

"But it is fortunate," thought she, "that have something to wish for.
Were the whole arrangement complete, my disappointment would be certain.
But here, by carrying with me one ceaseless source of regret in my
sister's absence, may reasonably hope to have all my expectations of
pleasure realized. scheme of which every part promises delight, can
never be successful; and general disappointment is only warded off by
the defence of some little peculiar vexation."

The park was very large, and contained great variety of ground. They

— listened, wondered, doubted, and was impatient for more. Mrs.

"This fine account of him," whispered her aunt, as they walked, "is not

In the gallery there were many family portraits, but they could have

There was certainly at this moment, in —'s mind, more gentle

much of pleasure or pain it was in his power to much of

When all of the house that was open to general inspection had been seen,
they returned down stairs, and taking leave of the housekeeper, were
consigned over to the gardener, who met them at the hall door.

As they walked across the lawn towards the river, — turned back
to look again; her uncle and aunt stopped also, and while the former was
conjecturing as to the date of the building, the owner of it himself
suddenly came forward from the road, which led behind it to the stables.

They were within twenty yards of each other, and so abrupt was his
appearance, that it was impossible to avoid his sight. Their eyes
instantly met, and the cheeks of each were overspread with the deepest
blush. He absolutely started, and for moment seemed immoveable from
surprise; but shortly recovering himself, advanced towards the party,
and spoke to —, if not in terms of perfect composure, at least
of perfect civility.

She had instinctively turned away; but, stopping on his approach,
received his compliments with an embarrassment impossible to be
overcome. Had his first appearance, or his resemblance to the picture
they had just been examining, been insufficient to assure the other two
that they now saw Mr. —, the gardener's expression of surprise, on
beholding his master, must immediately have told it. They stood little
aloof while he was talking to their niece, who, astonished and confused,
scarcely dared lift her eyes to his face, and knew not what answer she
returned to his civil enquiries after her family. Amazed at the
alteration in his manner since they last parted, every sentence that he
uttered was increasing her embarrassment; and every idea of the
impropriety of her being found there, recurring to her mind, the few
minutes in which they continued together, were some of the most
uncomfortable of her life. Nor did he seem much more at ease; when he
spoke, his accent had none of its usual sedateness; and he repeated his
enquiries as to the time of her having left —, and of her stay
in —, so often, and in so hurried way, as plainly spoke the
distraction of his thoughts.

At length, every idea seemed to fail him; and, after standing few
moments without saying word, he suddenly recollected himself, and took
leave.

The others then joined her, and expressed their admiration of his
figure; but — heard not word, and, wholly engrossed by her own
feelings, followed them in silence. She was overpowered by shame and
vexation. Her coming there was the most unfortunate, the most ill-judged
thing in the world! How strange must it appear to him! In what
disgraceful light might it not strike so vain man! It might seem as if
she had purposely thrown herself in his way again! Oh! why did she come?
or, why did he thus come day before he was expected? Had they been
only ten minutes sooner, they should have been beyond the reach of his
discrimination, for it was plain that he was that moment arrived, that
moment alighted from his horse or his carriage. She blushed again and
again over the perverseness of the meeting. — his behaviour, so
strikingly could it mean? That he should even speak to
her was to speak with such civility, to enquire after her
family! Never in her life had she seen his manners so little dignified,
never had he spoken with such gentleness as on this unexpected meeting.
What contrast did it offer to his last address in —'s —, when
he put his letter into her hand! She knew not what to think, nor how to
account for it.

They had now entered beautiful walk by the side of the water, and
every step was bringing forward nobler fall of ground, or finer
reach of the woods to which they were approaching; but it was some time
before — was sensible of any of it; and, though she answered
mechanically to the repeated appeals of her uncle and aunt, and seemed
to direct her eyes to such objects as they pointed out, she
distinguished no part of the scene. Her thoughts were all fixed on that
one spot of — —, whichever it might be, where Mr. — then
was. She longed to know what at that moment was passing in his mind; in
what manner he thought of her, and whether, in defiance of every thing,
she was still dear to him. Perhaps he had been civil, only because he
felt himself at ease; yet there had been _that_ in his voice, which was
not like ease. Whether he had felt more of pain or of pleasure in seeing
her, she could not tell, but he certainly had not seen her with
composure.

At length, however, the remarks of her companions on her absence of mind
roused her, and she felt the necessity of appearing more like herself.

They entered the woods, and bidding adieu to the river for while,
ascended some of the higher grounds; whence, in spots where the opening
of the trees gave the eye power to wander, were many charming views of
the valley, the opposite hills, with the long range of woods
overspreading many, and occasionally part of the stream. Mr. —
expressed wish of going round the whole —, but feared it might be
beyond walk. With triumphant smile, they were told, that it was ten
miles round. It settled the matter; and they pursued the accustomed
circuit; which brought them again, after some time, in descent among
hanging woods, to the edge of the water, in one of its narrowest parts.
They crossed it by simple bridge, in character with the general air of
the scene; it was spot less adorned than any they had yet visited; and
the valley, here contracted into glen, allowed room only for the
stream, and narrow walk amidst the rough coppice-wood which bordered
it. — longed to explore its windings; but when they had crossed
the bridge, and perceived their distance from the house, Mrs. —,
who was not great walker, could go no farther, and thought only of
returning to the carriage as quickly as possible. Her niece was,
therefore, obliged to submit, and they took their way towards the house
on the opposite side of the river, in the nearest direction; but their
progress was slow, for Mr. —, though seldom able to indulge the
taste, was very fond of fishing, and was so much engaged in watching the
occasional appearance of some trout in the water, and talking to the man
about them, that he advanced but little. Whilst wandering on in this
slow manner, they were again surprised, and —'s astonishment was
quite equal to what it had been at first, by the sight of Mr. —
approaching them, and at no great distance. The walk being here less
sheltered than on the other side, allowed them to see him before they
met. —, however astonished, was at least more prepared for an
interview than before, and resolved to appear and to speak with
calmness, if he really intended to meet them. For few moments, indeed,
she felt that he would probably strike into some other path. This idea
lasted while turning in the walk concealed him from their view; the
turning past, he was immediately before them. With glance she saw,
that he had lost none of his recent civility; and, to imitate his
politeness, she began, as they met, to admire the beauty of the place;
but she had not got beyond the words "delightful," and "charming," when
some unlucky recollections obtruded, and she fancied that praise of
— from her, might be mischievously construed. Her colour
changed, and she said no more.

Mrs. — was standing little behind; and on her pausing, he asked
her, if she would do him the honour of introducing him to her friends.
This was stroke of civility for which she was quite unprepared; and
she could hardly suppress smile, at his being now seeking the
acquaintance of some of those very people, against whom his pride had
revolted, in his offer to herself. "What will be his surprise," thought
she, "when he knows who they are! He takes them now for people of
fashion."

The introduction, however, was immediately made; and as she named their
relationship to herself, she stole sly look at him, to see how he bore
it; and was not without the expectation of his decamping as fast as he
could from such disgraceful companions. That he was _surprised_ by the
connexion was evident; he sustained it however with fortitude, and so
far from going away, turned back with them, and entered into
conversation with Mr. —. — could not but be pleased,
could not but triumph. It was consoling, that he should know she had
some relations for whom there was no need to blush. She listened most
attentively to all that passed between them, and gloried in every
expression, every sentence of her uncle, which marked his intelligence,
his taste, or his good manners.

The conversation soon turned upon fishing, and she heard Mr. —
invite him, with the greatest civility, to fish there as often as he
chose, while he continued in the neighbourhood, offering at the same
time to supply him with fishing tackle, and pointing out those parts of
the stream where there was usually most sport. Mrs. —, who was
walking arm in arm with —, gave her look expressive of her
wonder. — said nothing, but it gratified her exceedingly; the
compliment must be all for herself. Her astonishment, however, was
extreme; and continually was she repeating, "— is he so altered? From
what can it proceed? It cannot be for _me_, it cannot be for _my_ sake
that his manners are thus softened. My reproofs at — could not
work such change as this. It is impossible that he should still love
me."

After walking some time in this way, the two ladies in front, the two
gentlemen behind, on resuming their places, after descending to the
