the country." He acknowledged the truth of it all; and said that
business with his steward had occasioned his coming forward few hours
before the rest of the party with whom he had been travelling. "They
will join me early to-morrow," he continued, "and among them are some
who will claim an acquaintance with — and his sisters."

— answered only by slight bow. Her thoughts were instantly
driven back to the time when Mr. —'s name had been last mentioned
between them; and if she might judge from his complexion, _his_ mind was
not very differently engaged.

"There is also one other person in the party," he continued after
pause, "who more particularly wishes to be known to you allow
me, or do ask too much, to introduce my sister to your acquaintance
during your stay at —?"

The surprise of such an application was great indeed; it was too great
for her to know in what manner she acceded to it. She immediately felt
that whatever desire — — might have of being acquainted with her,
must be the work of her brother, and without looking farther, it was
satisfactory; it was gratifying to know that his resentment had not made
him think really ill of her.

They now walked on in silence; each of them deep in thought. —
was not comfortable; that was impossible; but she was flattered and
pleased. His wish of introducing his sister to her, was compliment of
the highest kind. They soon outstripped the others, and when they had
reached the carriage, Mr. and Mrs. — were half quarter of
mile behind.

He then asked her to walk into the she declared herself not
tired, and they stood together on the lawn. At such time, much might
have been said, and silence was very awkward. She wanted to talk, but
there seemed an embargo on every subject. At last she recollected that
she had been travelling, and they talked of — and — — with
great perseverance. Yet time and her aunt moved her patience
and her ideas were nearly worn out before the was over. On
Mr. and Mrs. —'s coming up, they were all pressed to go into the
house and take some refreshment; but this was declined, and they parted
on each side with the utmost politeness. Mr. — handed the ladies
into the carriage, and when it drove off, — saw him walking
slowly towards the house.

The observations of her uncle and aunt now began; and each of them
pronounced him to be infinitely superior to any thing they had expected.
"He is perfectly well behaved, polite, and unassuming," said her uncle.

"There _is_ something little stately in him to be sure," replied her
aunt, "but it is confined to his air, and is not unbecoming. can now
say with the housekeeper, that though some people may call him proud,
have seen nothing of it."

was never more surprised than by his behaviour to us. It was more
than civil; it was really attentive; and there was no necessity for such
attention. His acquaintance with — was very trifling."

"To be sure, —," said her aunt, "he is not so handsome as —;
or rather he has not —'s countenance, for his features are
perfectly good. But how came you to tell us that he was so
disagreeable?"

— excused herself as well as she could; said that she had liked
him better when they met in — than before, and that she had never
seen him so pleasant as this morning.

"But perhaps he may be little whimsical in his civilities," replied
her uncle. "Your great men often are; and therefore shall not take him
at his word about fishing, as he might change his mind another day, and
warn me off his grounds."

— felt that they had entirely mistaken his character, but said
nothing.

"From what we have seen of him," continued Mrs. —, really
should not have thought that he could have behaved in so cruel way by
any body, as he has done by poor —. He has not an ill-natured
look. On the contrary, there is something pleasing about his mouth when
he speaks. — there is something of dignity in his countenance, that
would not give one an unfavourable idea of his heart. But to be sure,
the good lady who shewed us the house, did give him most flaming
character! could hardly help laughing aloud sometimes. But he is
liberal master, suppose, and _that_ in the eye of servant
comprehends every virtue."

— here felt herself called on to say something in vindication of
his behaviour to —; and therefore gave them to understand, in as
guarded manner as she could, that by what she had heard from his
relations in —, his actions were capable of very different
construction; and that his character was by no means so faulty, nor
—'s so amiable, as they had been considered in —. In
confirmation of this, she related the particulars of all the pecuniary
transactions in which they had been connected, without actually naming
her authority, but stating it to be such as might be relied on.

Mrs. — was surprised and concerned; but as they were now
approaching the scene of her former pleasures, every idea gave way to
the charm of recollection; and she was too much engaged in pointing out
to her husband all the interesting spots in its environs, to think of
any thing else. Fatigued as she had been by the morning's walk, they had
no sooner dined than she set off again in quest of her former
acquaintance, and the evening was spent in the satisfactions of an
intercourse renewed after many years discontinuance.

The occurrences of the day were too full of interest to leave —
much attention for any of these new friends; and she could do nothing
but think, and think with wonder, of Mr. —'s civility, and above
all, of his wishing her to be acquainted with his sister.

— had settled it that Mr. — would bring his sister to visit
her, the very day after her reaching —; and was consequently
resolved not to be out of sight of the inn the whole of that morning.
But her conclusion was false; for on the very morning after their own
arrival at —, these visitors came. They had been walking about the
place with some of their new friends, and were just returned to the inn
to dress themselves for dining with the same family, when the sound of
carriage drew them to window, and they saw gentleman and lady in
curricle, driving up the street. — immediately recognising the
livery, guessed what it meant, and imparted no small degree of surprise
to her relations, by acquainting them with the honour which she
expected. Her uncle and aunt were all amazement; and the embarrassment
of her manner as she spoke, joined to the circumstance itself, and many
of the circumstances of the preceding day, opened to them new idea on
the business. Nothing had ever suggested it before, but they now felt
that there was no other way of accounting for such attentions from such
quarter, than by supposing partiality for their niece. While these
newly-born notions were passing in their heads, the perturbation of
—'s feelings was every moment increasing. She was quite amazed
at her own discomposure; but amongst other causes of disquiet, she
dreaded lest the partiality of the brother should have said too much in
her favour; and more than commonly anxious to please, she naturally
suspected that every power of pleasing would fail her.

She retreated from the window, fearful of being seen; and as she walked
up and down the room, endeavouring to compose herself, saw such looks
of enquiring surprise in her uncle and aunt, as made every thing worse.

— — and her brother appeared, and this formidable introduction
took place. With astonishment did — see, that her new
acquaintance was at least as much embarrassed as herself. Since her
being at —, she had heard that — — was exceedingly proud;
but the observation of very few minutes convinced her, that she was
only exceedingly shy. She found it difficult to obtain even word from
her beyond monosyllable.

— — was tall, and on larger scale than —; and, though
little more than sixteen, her figure was formed, and her appearance
womanly and graceful. She was less handsome than her brother, but there
was sense and good humour in her face, and her manners were perfectly
unassuming and gentle. —, who had expected to find in her as
acute and unembarrassed an observer as ever Mr. — had been, was much
relieved by discerning such different feelings.

They had not been long together, before — told her that — was
also coming to wait on her; and she had barely time to express her
satisfaction, and prepare for such visitor, when —'s quick step
was heard on the stairs, and in moment he entered the room. All
—'s anger against him had been long done away; but, had she
still felt any, it could hardly have stood its ground against the
unaffected cordiality with which he expressed himself, on seeing her
again. He enquired in friendly, though general way, after her family,
and looked and spoke with the same good-humoured ease that he had ever
done.

To Mr. and Mrs. — he was scarcely less interesting personage
than to herself. They had long wished to see him. The whole party before
them, indeed, excited lively attention. The suspicions which had just
arisen of Mr. — and their niece, directed their observation towards
each with an earnest, though guarded, enquiry; and they soon drew from
those enquiries the full conviction that one of them at least knew what
it was to love. Of the lady's sensations they remained little in
doubt; but that the gentleman was overflowing with admiration was
evident enough.

—, on her side, had much to do. She wanted to ascertain the
feelings of each of her visitors, she wanted to compose her own, and to
make herself agreeable to all; and in the latter object, where she
feared most to fail, she was most sure of success, for those to whom she
endeavoured to give pleasure were prepossessed in her favour. —
was ready, — was eager, and — determined, to be pleased.

In seeing —, her thoughts naturally flew to her sister; and oh!
how ardently did she long to know, whether any of his were directed in
like manner. Sometimes she could fancy, that he talked less than on
former occasions, and once or twice pleased herself with the notion that
as he looked at her, he was trying to trace resemblance. But, though
this might be imaginary, she could not be deceived as to his behaviour
to — —, who had been set up as rival of —. No look appeared
on either side that spoke particular regard. Nothing occurred between
them that could justify the hopes of his sister. On this point she was
soon satisfied; and two or three little circumstances occurred ere they
parted, which, in her anxious interpretation, denoted recollection of
—, not untinctured by tenderness, and wish of saying more that
might lead to the mention of her, had he dared. He observed to her, at
moment when the others were talking together, and in tone which had
something of real regret, that it "was very long time since he had had
the pleasure of seeing her;" and, before she could reply, he added, "—
is above eight months. We have not met since the 26th of —, when
we were all dancing together at —."

— was pleased to find his memory so exact; and he afterwards
took occasion to ask her, when unattended to by any of the rest,
whether _all_ her sisters were at —. There was not much in the
question, nor in the preceding remark, but there was look and manner
which gave them meaning.

It was not often that she could turn her eyes on Mr. — himself; but,
whenever she did catch glimpse, she saw an expression of general
complaisance, and in all that he said, she heard an accent so far
removed from hauteur or disdain of his companions, as convinced her that
the improvement of manners which she had yesterday witnessed, however
temporary its existence might prove, had at least outlived one day. When
she saw him thus seeking the acquaintance, and courting the good opinion
of people, with whom any intercourse few months ago would have been
disgrace; when she saw him thus civil, not only to herself, but to the
very relations whom he had openly disdained, and recollected their last
lively scene in — —, the difference, the change was so
great, and struck so forcibly on her mind, that she could hardly
restrain her astonishment from being visible. Never, even in the company
of his dear friends at —, or his dignified relations at
—, had she seen him so desirous to please, so free from
self-consequence, or unbending reserve as now, when no importance could
result from the success of his endeavours, and when even the
acquaintance of those to whom his attentions were addressed, would draw
down the ridicule and censure of the ladies both of — and
—.

Their visitors staid with them above half an hour, and when they arose
to depart, Mr. — called on his sister to join him in expressing
their wish of seeing Mr. and Mrs. —, and — —, to dinner
at —, before they left the country. — —, though with
diffidence which marked her little in the habit of giving invitations,
readily obeyed. Mrs. — looked at her niece, desirous of knowing
how _she_, whom the invitation most concerned, felt disposed as to its
acceptance, but — had turned away her head. Presuming, however,
that this studied avoidance spoke rather momentary embarrassment, than
any dislike of the proposal, and seeing in her husband, who was fond of
society, perfect willingness to accept it, she ventured to engage for
her attendance, and the day after the next was fixed on.

— expressed great pleasure in the certainty of seeing —
again, having still great deal to say to her, and many enquiries to
make after all their — friends. —, construing all
this into wish of hearing her speak of her sister, was pleased; and on
this account, as well as some others, found herself, when their visitors
left them, capable of considering the last half hour with some
satisfaction, though while it was passing, the enjoyment of it had been
little. Eager to be alone, and fearful of enquiries or hints from her
uncle and aunt, she staid with them only long enough to hear their
favourable opinion of —, and then hurried away to dress.

But she had no reason to fear Mr. and Mrs. —'s curiosity; it was
not their wish to force her communication. It was evident that she was
much better acquainted with Mr. — than they had before any idea of;
it was evident that he was very much in love with her. They saw much to
interest, but nothing to justify enquiry.

Of Mr. — it was now matter of anxiety to think well; and, as far
as their acquaintance reached, there was no fault to find. They could
not be untouched by his politeness, and had they drawn his character
from their own feelings, and his servant's report, without any reference
to any other account, the circle in — to which he was known,
would not have recognised it for Mr. —. There was now an interest,
however, in believing the housekeeper; and they soon became sensible,
that the authority of servant who had known him since he was four
years old, and whose own manners indicated respectability, was not to be
hastily rejected. Neither had any thing occurred in the intelligence of
their — friends, that could materially lessen its weight. They
had nothing to accuse him of but pride; pride he probably had, and if
not, it would certainly be imputed by the inhabitants of small
market-town, where the family did not visit. It was acknowledged,
however, that he was liberal man, and did much good among the poor.

With respect to —, the travellers soon found that he was not held
there in much estimation; for though the chief of his concerns, with the
son of his patron, were imperfectly understood, it was yet well known
fact that, on his quitting —, he had left many debts behind
him, which Mr. — afterwards discharged.

As for —, her thoughts were at — this evening more than
the last; and the evening, though as it passed it seemed long, was not
long enough to determine her feelings towards _one_ in that mansion; and
she lay awake two whole hours, endeavouring to make them out. She
certainly did not hate him. No; hatred had vanished long ago, and she
had almost as long been ashamed of ever feeling dislike against him,
that could be so called. The respect created by the conviction of his
valuable qualities, though at first unwillingly admitted, had for some
time ceased to be repugnant to her feelings; and it was now heightened
into somewhat of friendlier nature, by the testimony so highly in his
favour, and bringing forward his disposition in so amiable light,
which yesterday had produced. But above all, above respect and esteem,
there was motive within her of good will which could not be
overlooked. It was not merely for having once
loved her, but for loving her still well enough, to forgive all the
petulance and acrimony of her manner in rejecting him, and all the
unjust accusations accompanying her rejection. He who, she had been
persuaded, would avoid her as his greatest enemy, seemed, on this
accidental meeting, most eager to preserve the acquaintance, and without
any indelicate display of regard, or any peculiarity of manner, where
their two selves only were concerned, was soliciting the good opinion
of her friends, and bent on making her known to his sister. Such
change in man of so much pride, excited not only astonishment but
to love, ardent love, it must be attributed; and as such
its impression on her was of sort to be encouraged, as by no means
unpleasing, though it could not be exactly defined. She respected, she
esteemed, she was grateful to him, she felt real interest in his
welfare; and she only wanted to know how far she wished that welfare to
depend upon herself, and how far it would be for the happiness of both
that she should employ the power, which her fancy told her she still
possessed, of bringing on the renewal of his addresses.

It had been settled in the evening, between the aunt and niece, that
such striking civility as — —'s, in coming to them on the very
day of her arrival at —, for she had reached it only to late
breakfast, ought to be imitated, though it could not be equalled, by
some exertion of politeness on their side; and, consequently, that it
would be highly expedient to wait on her at — the following
morning. They were, therefore, to was pleased, though,
when she asked herself the reason, she had very little to say in reply.

Mr. — left them soon after breakfast. The fishing scheme had been
renewed the day before, and positive engagement made of his meeting
some of the gentlemen at — by noon.

Convinced as — now was that — —'s dislike of her had
originated in jealousy, she could not help feeling how very unwelcome
her appearance at — must be to her, and was curious to know with
how much civility on that lady's side, the acquaintance would now be
renewed.

On reaching the house, they were shewn through the hall into the saloon,
whose northern aspect rendered it delightful for summer. Its windows
opening to the ground, admitted most refreshing view of the high woody
hills behind the house, and of the beautiful oaks and — chesnuts
which were scattered over the intermediate lawn.

In this room they were received by — —, who was sitting there
with Mrs. — and — —, and the lady with whom she lived in
—. —'s reception of them was very civil; but attended with
all that embarrassment which, though proceeding from shyness and the
fear of doing wrong, would easily give to those who felt themselves
inferior, the belief of her being proud and reserved. Mrs. — and
her niece, however, did her justice, and pitied her.

By Mrs. — and — —, they were noticed only by curtsey; and
on their being seated, pause, awkward as such pauses must always be,
succeeded for few moments. It was first broken by Mrs. —,
genteel, agreeable-looking woman, whose endeavour to introduce some kind
of discourse, proved her to be more truly well bred than either of the
others; and between her and Mrs. —, with occasional help from
—, the conversation was carried on. — — looked as if she
wished for courage enough to join in it; and sometimes did venture
short sentence, when there was least danger of its being heard.

— soon saw that she was herself closely watched by — —,
and that she could not speak word, especially to — —, without
calling her attention. This observation would not have prevented her
from trying to talk to the latter, had they not been seated at an
inconvenient distance; but she was not sorry to be spared the necessity
of saying much. Her own thoughts were employing her. She expected every
moment that some of the gentlemen would enter the room. She wished, she
feared that the master of the house might be amongst them; and whether
she wished or feared it most, she could scarcely determine. After
sitting in this manner quarter of an hour, without hearing —
—'s voice, — was roused by receiving from her cold
enquiry after the health of her family. She answered with equal
indifference and brevity, and the other said no more.

The next variation which their visit afforded was produced by the
entrance of servants with cold meat, cake, and variety of all the
finest fruits in season; but this did not take place till after many
significant look and smile from Mrs. — to — — had been
given, to remind her of her post. There was now employment for the whole
party; for though they could not all talk, they could all eat; and the
beautiful pyramids of grapes, nectarines, and peaches, soon collected
them round the table.

While thus engaged, — had fair opportunity of deciding whether
she most feared or wished for the appearance of Mr. —, by the
feelings which prevailed on his entering the room; and then, though but
moment before she had believed her wishes to predominate, she began to
regret that he came.

He had been some time with Mr. —, who, with two or three other
gentlemen from the house, was engaged by the river, and had left him
only on learning that the ladies of the family intended visit to
— that morning. No sooner did he appear, than — wisely
resolved to be perfectly easy and resolution the more
necessary to be made, but perhaps not the more easily kept, because she
saw that the suspicions of the whole party were awakened against them,
and that there was scarcely an eye which did not watch his behaviour
when he first came into the room. In no countenance was attentive
curiosity so strongly marked as in — —'s, in spite of the
smiles which overspread her face whenever she spoke to one of its
objects; for jealousy had not yet made her desperate, and her attentions
to Mr. — were by no means over. — —, on her brother's
entrance, exerted herself much more to talk; and — saw that he
was anxious for his sister and herself to get acquainted, and forwarded,
as much as possible, every attempt at conversation on either side. —
— saw all this likewise; and, in the imprudence of anger, took the
first opportunity of saying, with sneering civility,

"Pray, — —, are not the ----shire militia removed from —?
They must be great loss to _your_ family."

In —'s presence she dared not mention —'s name; but —
instantly comprehended that he was uppermost in her thoughts; and the
various recollections connected with him gave her moment's distress;
but, exerting herself vigorously to repel the ill-natured attack, she
presently answered the question in tolerably disengaged tone. While
she spoke, an involuntary glance shewed her — with an heightened
complexion, earnestly looking at her, and his sister overcome with
confusion, and unable to lift up her eyes. Had — — known what
pain she was then giving her beloved friend, she undoubtedly would have
refrained from the hint; but she had merely intended to discompose
—, by bringing forward the idea of man to whom she believed
her partial, to make her betray sensibility which might injure her in
—'s opinion, and perhaps to remind the latter of all the follies and
absurdities, by which some part of her family were connected with that
corps. Not syllable had ever reached her of — —'s meditated
elopement. To no creature had it been revealed, where secresy was
possible, except to —; and from all —'s connections her
brother was particularly anxious to conceal it, from that very wish
which — had long ago attributed to him, of their becoming
hereafter her own. He had certainly formed such plan, and without
meaning that it should affect his endeavour to separate him from —
—, it is probable that it might add something to his lively concern
for the welfare of his friend.

—'s collected behaviour, however, soon quieted his emotion; and
as — —, vexed and disappointed, dared not approach nearer to
—, — also recovered in time, though not enough to be able
to speak any more. Her brother, whose eye she feared to meet, scarcely
recollected her interest in the affair, and the very circumstance which
had been designed to turn his thoughts from —, seemed to have
fixed them on her more, and more cheerfully.

Their visit did not continue long after the question and answer
above-mentioned; and while Mr. — was attending them to their
carriage, — — was venting her feelings in criticisms on
—'s person, behaviour, and dress. But — would not join
her. Her brother's recommendation was enough to ensure her favour: his
judgment could not err, and he had spoken in such terms of —, as
to leave — without the power of finding her otherwise than
lovely and amiable. When — returned to the saloon, — —
could not help repeating to him some part of what she had been saying to
his sister.

"How very ill — — looks this morning, Mr. —," she cried;
never in my life saw any one so much altered as she is since the winter.
She is grown so brown and coarse! — and were agreeing that we
should not have known her again."

However little Mr. — might have liked such an address, he contented
himself with coolly replying, that he perceived no other alteration than
her being rather miraculous consequence of travelling in the
summer.

"For my own part," she rejoined, must confess that never could see
any beauty in her. Her face is too thin; her complexion has no
brilliancy; and her features are not at all handsome. Her nose wants
character; there is nothing marked in its lines. Her teeth are
tolerable, but not out of the common way; and as for her eyes, which
have sometimes been called so fine, never could perceive any thing
extraordinary in them. They have sharp, shrewish look, which do not
like at all; and in her air altogether, there is self-sufficiency
without fashion, which is intolerable."

Persuaded as — — was that — admired —, this was not
the best method of recommending herself; but angry people are not always
wise; and in seeing him at last look somewhat nettled, she had all the
success she expected. He was resolutely silent however; and, from
determination of making him speak, she continued,

remember, when we first knew her in —, how amazed we all
were to find that she was reputed beauty; and particularly recollect
your saying one night, after they had been dining at —, '_—_
should as soon call her mother wit.' — afterwards she
seemed to improve on you, and believe you thought her rather pretty at
one time."

"Yes," replied —, who could contain himself no longer, "but _that_
was only when first knew her, for it is many months since have
considered her as one of the handsomest women of my acquaintance."

He then went away, and — — was left to all the satisfaction of
having forced him to say what gave no one any pain but herself.

Mrs. — and — talked of all that had occurred, during
their visit, as they returned, except what had particularly interested
them both. The looks and behaviour of every body they had seen were
discussed, except of the person who had mostly engaged their attention.
They talked of his sister, his friends, his house, his fruit, of every
thing but himself; yet — was longing to know what Mrs. —
thought of him, and Mrs. — would have been highly gratified by
her niece's beginning the subject.

— had been good deal disappointed in not finding letter from
—, on their first arrival at —; and this disappointment had
been renewed on each of the mornings that had now been spent there; but
on the third, her repining was over, and her sister justified by the
receipt of two letters from her at once, on one of which was marked that
it had been missent elsewhere. — was not surprised at it, as
— had written the direction remarkably ill.

They had just been preparing to walk as the letters came in; and her
uncle and aunt, leaving her to enjoy them in quiet, set off by
themselves. The one missent must be first attended to; it had been
written five days ago. The beginning contained an account of all their
little parties and engagements, with such news as the country afforded;
but the latter half, which was dated day later, and written in evident
agitation, gave more important intelligence. It was to this effect:

"Since writing the above, dearest —, something has occurred of
most unexpected and serious nature; but am afraid of alarming
assured that we are all well. What have to say relates to
poor —. An express came at twelve last night, just as we were
all gone to bed, from — —, to inform us that she was
gone off to — with one of his officers; to own the truth,
with —!--— our surprise. To —, however, it does not
seem so wholly unexpected. am very, very sorry. So imprudent
match on both am willing to hope the best, and that
his character has been misunderstood. Thoughtless and indiscreet
can easily believe him, but this step (and let us rejoice over it)
marks nothing bad at heart. His choice is disinterested at least,
for he must know my father can give her nothing. Our poor mother
is sadly grieved. My father bears it better. How thankful am
that we never let them know what has been said against him; we must
forget it ourselves. They were off — night about twelve, as
is conjectured, but were not missed till yesterday morning at
eight. The express was sent off directly. My dear —, they must
have passed within ten miles of us. — — gives us reason
to expect him here soon. — left few lines for his wife,
informing her of their intention. must conclude, for cannot be
long from my poor mother. am afraid you will not be able to make
it out, but hardly know what have written."

Without allowing herself time for consideration, and scarcely knowing
what she felt, — on finishing this letter, instantly seized the
other, and opening it with the utmost impatience, read as follows: it
had been written day later than the conclusion of the first.

"By this time, my dearest sister, you have received my hurried
letter; wish this may be more intelligible, but though not
confined for time, my head is so bewildered that cannot answer
for being coherent. —arest —, hardly know what would
write, but have bad news for you, and it cannot be delayed.
Imprudent as marriage between Mr. — and our poor —
would be, we are now anxious to be assured it has taken place, for
there is but too much reason to fear they are not gone to —.
— — came yesterday, having left — the day
before, not many hours after the express. Though —'s short
letter to Mrs. gave them to understand that they were going to
— —, something was dropped by — expressing his belief
that never intended to go there, or to marry — at all, which
was repeated to — who instantly taking the alarm, set off
from intending to trace their route. He did trace them easily to
—, but no farther; for on entering that place they removed
into hackney-coach and dismissed the chaise that brought them
from —. All that is known after this is, that they were seen
to continue the — road. know not what to think. After making
every possible enquiry on that side —, — came on into
—, anxiously renewing them at all the turnpikes, and at
the inns in — and —, but without any success, no such
people had been seen to pass through. With the kindest concern he
came on to —, and broke his apprehensions to us in manner
most creditable to his heart. am sincerely grieved for him and
Mrs. but no one can throw any blame on them. Our distress, my
dear —, is very great. My father and mother believe the worst,
but cannot think so ill of him. —ny circumstances might make it
more eligible for them to be married privately in town than to

— hesitated, but her knees trembled under her, and she felt how

On his quitting the room, she sat down, unable to support herself, and

present glass of get you are very
ill."

She burst into tears as she alluded to it, and for few minutes could

— made no answer. He seemed scarcely to hear her, and was walking up

But self, though it would intrude, could not engross her. —--the

He readily assured her of his expressed his sorrow for

As he quitted the room, — felt how improbable it was that they
should ever see each other again on such terms of cordiality as had
marked their several meetings in —; and as she threw
retrospective glance over the whole of their acquaintance, so full of
contradictions and varieties, sighed at the perverseness of those
feelings which would now have promoted its continuance, and would
formerly have rejoiced in its termination.

If gratitude and esteem are good foundations of affection, —'s
change of sentiment will be neither improbable nor faulty. But if
otherwise, if the regard springing from such sources is unreasonable or
unnatural, in comparison of what is so often described as arising on
first interview with its object, and even before two words have been
exchanged, nothing can be said in her defence, except that she had given
somewhat of trial to the latter method, in her partiality for —,
and that its ill-success might perhaps authorise her to seek the other
less interesting mode of attachment. Be that as it may, she saw him go
with regret; and in this early example of what —'s infamy must

hope of —'s meaning to marry her. No one but —, she thought,
could flatter herself with such an expectation. Surprise was the least

letter remained on her mind, she was all astonishment that
— should marry girl, whom it was impossible he could marry for

as this, she might have sufficient charms; and though she did not
suppose — to be deliberately engaging in an elopement, without the
intention of marriage, she had no difficulty in believing that neither
her virtue nor her understanding would preserve her from falling an easy
prey.

She had never perceived, while the regiment was in —, that
— had any partiality for him, but she was convinced that — had

officer, sometimes another had been her favourite, as their attentions
raised them in her opinion. Her affections had been continually
fluctuating, but never without an object. The mischief of neglect and
mistaken indulgence towards such how acutely did she now
feel it.

She was wild to be at hear, to see, to be upon the spot, to

But wishes were vain; or at best could serve only to amuse her in the

have been thinking it over again, —," said her uncle, as they

It may be easily believed, that however little of novelty could be added

no interval of ease or forgetfulness.

They travelled as expeditiously as possible; and sleeping one night on
the road, reached — by dinner-time the next day. It was
comfort to — to consider that — could not have been wearied

The little —, attracted by the sight of chaise, were standing

— jumped out; and, after giving each of them an hasty kiss,

—, as she affectionately embraced her, whilst tears filled the

"Not yet," replied —. "But now that my dear uncle is come, hope
every thing will be well."

"We have heard only once. He wrote me few lines on —, to say

"My mother is tolerably well, trust; though her spirits are greatly

Her sister, however, assured her, of her being perfectly well; and their

When they were all in the drawing-room, the questions which —
had already asked, were of course repeated by the others, and they soon
found that — had no intelligence to give. The sanguine hope of good,
however, which the benevolence of her heart suggested, had not yet
deserted her; she still expected that it would all end well, and that
every morning would bring some letter, either from — or her father,
to explain their proceedings, and perhaps announce the marriage.

Mrs. —, to whose apartment they all repaired, after few minutes
conversation together, received them exactly as might be expected; with
tears and lamentations of regret, invectives against the villanous
conduct of —, and complaints of her own sufferings and ill usage;
blaming every body but the person to whose ill judging indulgence the
errors of her daughter must be principally owing.

"If had been able," said she, "to carry my point of going to —,
with all my family, _this_ would not have happened; but poor dear —
had nobody to take care of her. Why did the — ever let her go out
of their sight? am sure there was some great neglect or other on their
side, for she is not the kind of girl to do such thing, if she had
been well looked after. always thought they were very unfit to have
the charge of her; but was over-ruled, as always am. Poor dear
child! — now here's Mr. — gone away, and know he will fight
—, wherever he meets him, and then he will be killed, and what is
to become of us all? The — will turn us out, before he is cold
in his grave; and if you are not kind to us, brother, do not know what
we shall do."

They all exclaimed against such terrific ideas; and Mr. —, after
general assurances of his affection for her and all her family, told
her that he meant to be in — the very next day, and would assist
Mr. — in every endeavour for recovering —.

"Do not give way to useless alarm," added he, "though it is right to be
prepared for the worst, there is no occasion to look on it as certain.
It is not quite week since they left —. In few days more, we
may gain some news of them, and till we know that they are not married,
and have no design of marrying, do not let us give the matter over as
lost. As soon as get to town, shall go to my brother, and make him
come home with me to — —, and then we may consult
together as to what is to be done."

"Oh! my dear brother," replied Mrs. —, "that is exactly what
could most wish for. — now do, when you get to town, find them out,
wherever they may be; and if they are not married already, _make_ them

fighting. Tell him what dreadful state am am frightened
out of my wits; and have such tremblings, such flutterings, all over me,

me, for she does not know which are the best warehouses. Oh, brother,
how kind you are! know you will contrive it all."

But Mr. —, though he assured her again of his earnest endeavours
in the cause, could not avoid recommending moderation to her, as well in
her hopes as her fears; and, after talking with her in this manner till
dinner was on table, they left her to vent all her feelings on the
housekeeper, who attended, in the absence of her daughters.

Though her brother and sister were persuaded that there was no real
occasion for such seclusion from the family, they did not attempt to
oppose it, for they knew that she had not prudence enough to hold her
tongue before the servants, while they waited at table, and judged it
better that _one_ only of the household, and the one whom they could
most trust, should comprehend all her fears and solicitude on the
subject.

In the dining-room they were soon joined by — and —, who had been
too busily engaged in their separate apartments, to make their

was visible in either, except that the loss of her favourite sister, or
the anger which she had herself incurred in the business, had given

with countenance of grave reflection, soon after they were seated at

"This is most unfortunate affair; and will probably be much talked of.
But we must stem the tide of malice, and pour into the wounded bosoms of
each other, the balm of sisterly consolation."

Then, perceiving in — no inclination of replying, she added,
"— as the event must be for —, we may draw from it this useful
lesson; that loss of virtue in female is one false
step involves her in endless her reputation is no less
brittle than it is that she cannot be too much guarded
in her behaviour towards the undeserving of the other sex."

— lifted up her eyes in amazement, but was too much oppressed to

In the afternoon, the two elder — — were able to be for half an

"— — did own that he had often suspected some partiality,

must confess that he did not speak so well of — as he formerly

— then took it from her pocket-book, and gave it to —. These

"You will laugh when you know where am gone, and cannot help

"Oh! thoughtless, thoughtless —!" cried — when she had

never saw any one so shocked. He could not speak word for full ten

do not hope there to be guarded at such time, is

"She had better have stayed at home," cried —; "perhaps she
_meant_ well, but, under such misfortune as this, one cannot see too
little of one's neighbours. Assistance is impossible; condolence,
insufferable. Let them triumph over us at distance, and be satisfied."

She then proceeded to enquire into the measures which her father had
intended to pursue, while in town, for the recovery of his daughter.

"He meant, believe," replied —, "to go to —, the place where
they last changed horses, see the postilions, and try if any thing could
be made out from them. His principal object must be, to discover the
number of the hackney coach which took them from —. It had come
with fare from —; and as he thought the circumstance of
gentleman and lady's removing from one carriage into another, might be
remarked, he meant to make enquiries at —. If he could any how
discover at what house the coachman had before set down his fare, he
determined to make enquiries there, and hoped it might not be impossible
to find out the stand and number of the coach. do not know of any
other designs that he had formed: but he was in such hurry to be gone,
and his spirits so greatly discomposed, that had difficulty in finding
out even so much as this."

The whole party were in hopes of letter from Mr. — the next
morning, but the post came in without bringing single line from him.
His family knew him to be on all common occasions, most negligent and
dilatory correspondent, but at such time, they had hoped for exertion.
They were forced to conclude, that he had no pleasing intelligence to
send, but even of _that_ they would have been glad to be certain. Mr.
— had waited only for the letters before he set off.

When he was gone, they were certain at least of receiving constant
information of what was going on, and their uncle promised, at parting,
to prevail on Mr. — to return to —, as soon as he could, to
the great consolation of his sister, who considered it as the only
security for her husband's not being killed in duel.

Mrs. — and the children were to remain in — few
days longer, as the former thought her presence might be serviceable to
her nieces. She shared in their attendance on Mrs. —, and was
great comfort to them, in their hours of freedom. Their other aunt also
visited them frequently, and always, as she said, with the design of
cheering and heartening them up, though as she never came without
reporting some fresh instance of —'s extravagance or irregularity,
she seldom went away without leaving them more dispirited than she found
them.

All — seemed striving to blacken the man, who, but three months
before, had been almost an angel of light. He was declared to be in debt
to every tradesman in the place, and his intrigues, all honoured with
the title of seduction, had been extended into every tradesman's family.
Every body declared that he was the wickedest young man in the world;
and every body began to find out, that they had always distrusted the
appearance of his goodness. —, though she did not credit above
half of what was said, believed enough to make her former assurance of
her sister's ruin still more certain; and even —, who believed still
less of it, became almost hopeless, more especially as the time was now
come, when if they had gone to —, which she had never before
entirely despaired of, they must in all probability have gained some
news of them.

Mr. — left — on —; on —, his wife received
letter from him; it told them, that on his arrival, he had immediately
found out his brother, and persuaded him to come to — street.
That Mr. — had been to — and —, before his arrival, but
without gaining any satisfactory information; and that he was now
determined to enquire at all the principal hotels in town, as Mr. —
thought it possible they might have gone to one of them, on their first
coming to —, before they procured lodgings. Mr. — himself
did not expect any success from this measure, but as his brother was
eager in it, he meant to assist him in pursuing it. He added, that Mr.
— seemed wholly disinclined at present, to leave —, and
promised to write again very soon. There was also postscript to this

have written to — — to desire him to find out, if

— was at no loss to understand from whence this deference for
her authority proceeded; but it was not in her power to give any
information of so satisfactory nature, as the compliment deserved.

She had never heard of his having had any relations, except father and
mother, both of whom had been dead many years. It was possible, however,
that some of his companions in the ----shire, might be able to give more
information; and, though she was not very sanguine in expecting it, the
application was something to look forward to.

Every day at — was now day of anxiety; but the most anxious
part of each was when the post was expected. The arrival of letters was
the first grand object of every morning's impatience. Through letters,
whatever of good or bad was to be told, would be communicated, and every
succeeding day was expected to bring some news of importance.

But before they heard again from Mr. —, letter arrived for

had received directions to open all that came for him in his absence,

feel myself called upon, by our relationship, and my situation

suffering under, of which we were yesterday informed by letter

kind, because proceeding from cause which no time can remove. No
arguments shall be wanting on my part, that can alleviate so severe

— it is the more to be lamented, because there is reason to

to console yourself as much as possible, to throw off your unworthy
child from your affection for ever, and leave her to reap the
fruits of her own heinous offence.

Mr. — did not write again, till he had received an answer from
— —; and then he had nothing of pleasant nature to send.
It was not known that — had single relation, with whom he kept
up any connection, and it was certain that he had no near one living.
His former acquaintance had been numerous; but since he had been in the
militia, it did not appear that he was on terms of particular friendship
with any of them. There was no one therefore who could be pointed out,
as likely to give any news of him. — in the wretched state of his own

Mr. — added in his letter, that they might expect to see their

his intreaty that he would return to his family, and

"What, is he coming home, and without poor —!" she cried. "Sure he

and make him marry her, if he comes away?"

As Mrs. — began to wish to be at home, it was settled that she
and her children should go to —, at the same time that Mr. —

journey, and brought its master back to —.

Mrs. — went away in all the perplexity about — and her

His name had never been voluntarily mentioned before them by her niece;
and the kind of half-expectation which Mrs. — had formed, of

— had received none since her return, that could come from
—.

The present unhappy state of the family, rendered any other excuse for
the lowness of her spirits unnecessary; nothing, therefore, could be

one sleepless night out of two.

When Mr. — arrived, he had all the appearance of his usual
philosophic composure. He said as little as he had ever been in the
habit of saying; made no mention of the business that had taken him
away, and it was some time before his daughters had courage to speak of
it.

It was not till the afternoon, when he joined them at tea, that
— ventured to introduce the subject; and then, on her briefly

nothing of that. Who should suffer but myself? It has been my own doing,
and ought to feel it."

"You may well warn me against such an evil. Human nature is so prone to
fall into it! No, —, let me once in my life feel how much have
been to blame. am not afraid of being overpowered by the impression.
It will pass away soon enough."

"She is happy, then," said her father, drily; "and her residence there
will probably be of some duration."

Then, after short silence, he continued, "—, bear you no
ill-will for being justified in your advice to me last —, which,
considering the event, shews some greatness of mind."

They were interrupted by — —, who came to fetch her mother's
tea.

"This is parade," cried he, "which does one good; it gives such an
elegance to misfortune! Another day will do the same; will sit in
my library, in my night cap and powdering gown, and give as much trouble
as perhaps, may defer it, till — runs away."

am not going to run away, —," said —, fretfully; "if
should ever go to —, would behave better than —."

"_You_ go to —!--I would not trust you so near it as — —

minutes of every day in rational manner."

"Well, well," said he, "do not make yourself unhappy. If you are good
girl for the next ten years, will take you to review at the end of

Two days after Mr. —'s return, as — and — were walking

Away ran the girls, too eager to get in to have time for speech. They

"If you are looking for my master, ma'am, he is walking towards the

Upon this information, they instantly passed through the hall once more,

—, who was not so light, nor so much in the habit of running as

"Is it possible!" cried —, when she had finished. "Can it be

"— is not so undeserving, then, as we have thought him;" said her

Most earnestly did she then intreat him to lose no more time before he

Consider how important every moment is, in such case."

mean, that no man in his senses, would marry — on so slight

"Ten thousand pounds! — forbid! How is half such sum to be

Mr. — made no answer, and each of them, deep in thought, continued

"— they are really to be married!" cried —, as soon as they

comfort myself with thinking," replied —, "that he certainly would

"If we are ever able to learn what —'s debts have been," said

"We must endeavour to forget all that has passed on either side," said

they will settle so quietly, and live in so rational manner, as may in
time make their past imprudence forgotten."

"Their conduct has been such," replied —, "as neither you, nor

It now occurred to the girls that their mother was in all likelihood
perfectly ignorant of what had happened. They went to the library,
therefore, and asked their father, whether he would not wish them to
make it known to her. He was writing, and, without raising his head,
coolly replied,

— took the letter from his writing table, and they went up

communication would, therefore, do for all. After slight preparation
for good news, the letter was read aloud. Mrs. — could hardly

"My dear, dear —!" she cried: "— is delightful will

Her eldest daughter endeavoured to give some relief to the violence of

"For we must attribute this happy conclusion," she added, "in great

"Well," cried her mother, "it is all very right; who should do it but

She was then proceeding to all the particulars of calico, muslin, and
cambric, and would shortly have dictated some very plentiful orders, had
not —, though with some difficulty, persuaded her to wait, till her
father was at leisure to be consulted. One day's delay she observed,
would be of small importance; and her mother was too happy, to be quite
so obstinate as usual. Other schemes too came into her head.

will go to —," said she, "as soon as am dressed, and tell the
good, good news to my sister —. — as come back, can call on
— — and Mrs. —. —, run down and order the carriage. An
airing would do me great deal of good, am sure. Girls, can do any
thing for you in —? Oh! here comes —. My dear —, have you
heard the good news? — — is going to be married; and you shall
all have bowl of punch, to make merry at her wedding."

Mrs. — began instantly to express her joy. — received her
congratulations amongst the rest, and then, sick of this folly, took
refuge in her own room, that she might think with freedom.

Poor —'s situation must, at best, be bad enough; but that it was no
worse, she had need to be thankful. She felt it so; and though, in
looking forward, neither rational happiness nor worldly prosperity,
could be justly expected for her sister; in looking back to what they
had feared, only two hours ago, she felt all the advantages of what they
had gained.

Mr. — had very often wished, before this period of his life, that,
instead of spending his whole income, he had laid by an annual sum, for
the better provision of his children, and of his wife, if she survived
him. He now wished it more than ever. Had he done his duty in that
respect, — need not have been indebted to her uncle, for whatever of
honour or credit could now be purchased for her. The satisfaction of
prevailing on one of the most worthless young men in — — to be
her husband, might then have rested in its proper place.

He was seriously concerned, that cause of so little advantage to any
one, should be forwarded at the sole expence of his and
he was determined, if possible, to find out the extent of his
assistance, and to discharge the obligation as soon as he could.

When first Mr. — had married, economy was held to be perfectly
useless; for, of course, they were to have son. This son was to join
in cutting off the entail, as soon as he should be of age, and the widow
and younger children would by that means be provided for. Five daughters
successively entered the world, but yet the son was to come; and Mrs.
—, for many years after —'s birth, had been certain that he
would. This event had at last been despaired of, but it was then too
late to be saving. Mrs. — had no turn for economy, and her
husband's love of independence had alone prevented their exceeding their
income.

Five thousand pounds was settled by marriage articles on Mrs. — and
the children. But in what proportions it should be divided amongst the
latter, depended on the will of the parents. This was one point, with
regard to — at least, which was now to be settled, and Mr. —
could have no hesitation in acceding to the proposal before him. In
terms of grateful acknowledgment for the kindness of his brother, though
expressed most concisely, he then delivered on paper his perfect
approbation of all that was done, and his willingness to fulfil the
engagements that had been made for him. He had never before supposed
that, could — be prevailed on to marry his daughter, it would be
done with so little inconvenience to himself, as by the present
arrangement. He would scarcely be ten pounds a-year the loser, by the
hundred that was to be paid them; for, what with her board and pocket
allowance, and the continual presents in money, which passed to her,
through her mother's hands, —'s expences had been very little within
that sum.

That it would be done with such trifling exertion on his side, too, was
another very welcome surprise; for his chief wish at present, was to
have as little trouble in the business as possible. When the first
transports of rage which had produced his activity in seeking her were
over, he naturally returned to all his former indolence. His letter was
soon dispatched; for though dilatory in undertaking business, he was
quick in its execution. He begged to know farther particulars of what he
was indebted to his brother; but was too angry with —, to send any
message to her.

The good news quickly spread through the house; and with proportionate
speed through the neighbourhood. It was borne in the latter with decent
philosophy. To be sure it would have been more for the advantage of
conversation, had — — — come upon the town; or, as the
happiest alternative, been secluded from the world, in some distant farm
house. But there was much to be talked of, in marrying her; and the
good-natured wishes for her well-doing, which had proceeded before, from
all the spiteful old ladies in —, lost but little of their spirit
in this change of circumstances, because with such an husband, her
misery was considered certain.

It was fortnight since Mrs. — had been down stairs, but on this
happy day, she again took her seat at the head of her table, and in
spirits oppressively high. No sentiment of shame gave damp to her
triumph. The marriage of daughter, which had been the first object of
her wishes, since — was sixteen, was now on the point of
accomplishment, and her thoughts and her words ran wholly on those
attendants of elegant nuptials, fine muslins, new carriages, and
servants. She was busily searching through the neighbourhood for
proper situation for her daughter, and, without knowing or considering
what their income might be, rejected many as deficient in size and
importance.

"Haye-— might do," said she, "if the — would quit it, or the
great house at —, if the drawing-room were larger; but — is
too far off! could not bear to have her ten miles from me; and as for
— —, the attics are dreadful."

Her husband allowed her to talk on without interruption, while the
servants remained. But when they had withdrawn, he said to her, "Mrs.
—, before you take any, or all of these houses, for your son and
daughter, let us come to right understanding. Into _one_ house in this
neighbourhood, they shall never have admittance. will not encourage
the impudence of either, by receiving them at —."

long dispute followed this declaration; but Mr. — was firm: it
soon led to another; and Mrs. — found, with amazement and horror,
that her husband would not advance guinea to buy clothes for his
daughter. He protested that she should receive from him no mark of
affection whatever, on the occasion. Mrs. — could hardly comprehend
it. That his anger could be carried to such point of inconceivable
resentment, as to refuse his daughter privilege, without which her
marriage would scarcely seem valid, exceeded all that she could believe
possible. She was more alive to the disgrace, which the want of new
clothes must reflect on her daughter's nuptials, than to any sense of
shame at her eloping and living with —, fortnight before they
took place.

— was now most heartily sorry that she had, from the distress of
the moment, been led to make Mr. — acquainted with their fears for
her sister; for since her marriage would so shortly give the proper
termination to the elopement, they might hope to conceal its
unfavourable beginning, from all those who were not immediately on the
spot.

She had no fear of its spreading farther, through his means. There were
few people on whose secrecy she would have more confidently depended;
but at the same time, there was no one, whose knowledge of sister's
frailty would have mortified her so much. Not, however, from any fear of
disadvantage from it, individually to herself; for at any rate, there
seemed gulf impassable between them. Had —'s marriage been
concluded on the most honourable terms, it was not to be supposed that
Mr. — would connect himself with family, where to every other
objection would now be added, an alliance and relationship of the
nearest kind with the man whom he so justly scorned.

From such connection she could not wonder that he should shrink. The
wish of procuring her regard, which she had assured herself of his
feeling in —, could not in rational expectation survive such
blow as this. She was humbled, she was grieved; she repented, though she
hardly knew of what. She became jealous of his esteem, when she could no
longer hope to be benefited by it. She wanted to hear of him, when there
seemed the least chance of gaining intelligence. She was convinced that
she could have been happy with him; when it was no longer likely they
should meet.

What triumph for him, as she often thought, could he know that the
proposals which she had proudly spurned only four months ago, would now
have been gladly and gratefully received! He was as generous, she
doubted not, as the most generous of his sex. But while he was mortal,
there must be triumph.

She began now to comprehend that he was exactly the man, who, in
disposition and talents, would most suit her. His understanding and
temper, though unlike her own, would have answered all her wishes. It
was an union that must have been to the advantage of both; by her ease
and liveliness, his mind might have been softened, his manners improved,
and from his judgment, information, and knowledge of the world, she must
have received benefit of greater importance.

But no such happy marriage could now teach the admiring multitude what
connubial felicity really was. An union of different tendency, and
precluding the possibility of the other, was soon to be formed in their
family.

How — and — were to be supported in tolerable independence,
she could not imagine. But how little of permanent happiness could
belong to couple who were only brought together because their passions
were stronger than their virtue, she could easily conjecture.

Mr. — soon wrote again to his brother. To Mr. —'s
acknowledgments he briefly replied, with assurances of his eagerness to
promote the welfare of any of his family; and concluded with intreaties
that the subject might never be mentioned to him again. The principal
purport of his letter was to inform them, that Mr. — had resolved
on quitting the —.

"It was greatly my wish that he should do so," he added, "as soon
as his marriage was fixed on. — think you will agree with me,
in considering removal from that corps as highly advisable, both
on his account and my niece's. It is Mr. —'s intention to go
into the regulars; and, among his former friends, there are still
some who are able and willing to assist him in the army. He has the
promise of an ensigncy in — regiment, now quartered in
the —. It is an advantage to have it so far from this part of
the kingdom. He promises fairly, and hope among different people,
where they may each have character to preserve, they will both be
more prudent. have written to — —, to inform him of
our present arrangements, and to request that he will satisfy the
various creditors of Mr. — in and near —, with
assurances of speedy payment, for which have pledged myself. —
will you give yourself the trouble of carrying similar assurances
to his creditors in —, of whom shall subjoin list,
according to his information. He has given in all his debts; hope
at least he has not deceived us. Haggerston has our directions, and
all will be completed in week. They will then join his regiment,
unless they are first invited to —; and understand from
Mrs. —, that my niece is very desirous of seeing you all,
before she leaves the —. She is well, and begs to be dutifully
remembered to you and her

Mr. — and his daughters saw all the advantages of —'s removal
from the ----shire, as clearly as Mr. — could do. But Mrs.
—, was not so well pleased with it. —'s being settled in the
—, just when she had expected most pleasure and pride in her
company, for she had by no means given up her plan of their residing in
—, was severe disappointment; and besides, it was such
pity that — should be taken from regiment where she was acquainted
with every body, and had so many favourites.

"She is so fond of Mrs. —," said she, "it will be quite shocking
to send her away! — there are several of the young men, too, that she
likes very much. The officers may not be so pleasant in —
regiment."

His daughter's request, for such it might be considered, of being
admitted into her family again, before she set off for the —,
received at first an absolute negative. But — and —, who
agreed in wishing, for the sake of their sister's feelings and
consequence, that she should be noticed on her marriage by her parents,
urged him so earnestly, yet so rationally and so mildly, to receive her
and her husband at —, as soon as they were married, that he was
prevailed on to think as they thought, and act as they wished. — their
mother had the satisfaction of knowing, that she should be able to shew
her married daughter in the neighbourhood, before she was banished to
the —. When Mr. — wrote again to his brother, therefore, he
sent his permission for them to come; and it was settled, that as soon
as the ceremony was over, they should proceed to —. —
was surprised, however, that — should consent to such scheme,
and, had she consulted only her own inclination, any meeting with him
would have been the last object of her wishes.

Their sister's wedding day arrived; and — and — felt for her
probably more than she felt for herself. The carriage was sent to meet
them at and they were to return in it, by dinner-time. Their
arrival was dreaded by the elder — —; and — more especially,
who gave — the feelings which would have attended herself, had _she_
been the culprit, was wretched in the thought of what her sister must
endure.

They came. The family were assembled in the breakfast room, to receive
them. Smiles decked the face of Mrs. —, as the carriage drove up to
the door; her husband looked impenetrably grave; her daughters, alarmed,
anxious, uneasy.

—'s voice was heard in the vestibule; the door was thrown open, and
she ran into the room. Her mother stepped forwards, embraced her, and
welcomed her with rapture; gave her hand with an affectionate smile to
—, who followed his lady, and wished them both joy, with an
alacrity which shewed no doubt of their happiness.

Their reception from Mr. —, to whom they then turned, was not quite
so cordial. His countenance rather gained in austerity; and he scarcely
opened his lips. The easy assurance of the young couple, indeed, was
enough to provoke him. — was disgusted, and even — — was
shocked. — was — still; untamed, unabashed, wild, noisy, and
fearless. She turned from sister to sister, demanding their
congratulations, and when at length they all sat down, looked eagerly
round the room, took notice of some little alteration in it, and
observed, with laugh, that it was great while since she had been
there.

— was not at all more distressed than herself, but his manners
were always so pleasing, that had his character and his marriage been
exactly what they ought, his smiles and his easy address, while he
claimed their relationship, would have delighted them all. — had
not before believed him quite equal to such assurance; but she sat down,
resolving within herself, to draw no limits in future to the impudence
of an impudent man. _She_ blushed, and — blushed; but the cheeks of
the two who caused their confusion, suffered no variation of colour.

There was no want of discourse. The bride and her mother could neither
of them talk fast enough; and —, who happened to sit near
—, began enquiring after his acquaintance in that neighbourhood,
with good humoured ease, which she felt very unable to equal in her
replies. They seemed each of them to have the happiest memories in the
world. Nothing of the past was recollected with pain; and — led
voluntarily to subjects, which her sisters would not have alluded to for
the world.

"Only think of its being three months," she cried, "since went away;
it seems but fortnight declare; and yet there have been things
enough happened in the time. Good gracious! when went away, am sure
had no more idea of being married till came back again! though
thought it would be very good fun if was."

Her father lifted up his eyes. — was distressed. — looked
expressively at —; but she, who never heard nor saw any thing of
which she chose to be insensible, gaily continued, "—! mamma, do the
people here abouts know am married to-day? was afraid they might
not; and we overtook — — in his curricle, so was
determined he should know it, and so let down the side glass next to
him, and took off my glove, and let my hand just rest upon the window
frame, so that he might see the ring, and then bowed and smiled like
any thing."

— could bear it no longer. She got up, and ran out of the room;
and returned no more, till she heard them passing through the hall to
the dining-parlour. She then joined them soon enough to see —, with
anxious parade, walk up to her mother's right hand, and hear her say to
her eldest sister, "—! —, take your place now, and you must go
lower, because am married woman."

It was not to be supposed that time would give — that embarrassment,
from which she had been so wholly free at first. Her ease and good
spirits increased. She longed to see Mrs. —, the —, and all
their other neighbours, and to hear herself called "Mrs. —," by
each of them; and in the mean time, she went after dinner to shew her
ring and boast of being married, to Mrs. — and the two housemaids.

"Well, mamma," said she, when they were all returned to the breakfast
room, "and what do you think of my husband? Is not he charming man?
am sure my sisters must all envy me. only hope they may have half my
good luck. They must all go to —. That is the place to get
husbands. What pity it is, mamma, we did not all go."

"Very true; and if had my will, we should. But my dear —, don't
at all like your going such way off. Must it be so?"

"Oh, lord! is nothing in that. shall like it of all
things. You and papa, and my sisters, must come down and see us. We
shall be at — all the winter, and dare say there will be some
balls, and will take care to get good partners for them all."

"— then when you go away, you may leave one or two of my sisters
behind you; and dare say shall get husbands for them before the
winter is over."

thank you for my share of the favour," said —; "but do not
particularly like your way of getting husbands."

Their visitors were not to remain above ten days with them. Mr. —
had received his commission before he left —, and he was to join
his regiment at the end of fortnight.

No one but Mrs. —, regretted that their stay would be so short; and
she made the most of the time, by visiting about with her daughter, and
having very frequent parties at home. These parties were acceptable to
all; to avoid family circle was even more desirable to such as did
think, than such as did not.

—'s affection for —, was just what — had expected to
find it; not equal to —'s for him. She had scarcely needed her
present observation to be satisfied, from the reason of things, that
their elopement had been brought on by the strength of her love, rather
than by his; and she would have wondered why, without violently caring
for her, he chose to elope with her at all, had she not felt certain
that his flight was rendered necessary by distress of circumstances; and
if that were the case, he was not the young man to resist an opportunity
of having companion.

— was exceedingly fond of him. He was her dear — on every
occasion; no one was to be put in competition with him. He did every
thing best in the world; and she was sure he would kill more birds on
the first of —, than any body else in the country.

One morning, soon after their arrival, as she was sitting with her two

On such encouragement to ask, — was forced to put it out of her

But to live in ignorance on such point was impossible; or at least it
was impossible not to try for information. Mr. — had been at her

— had dropt, if it were compatible with the secrecy which had been

"You may readily comprehend," she added, "what my curiosity must be to

"Not that _shall_ though," she added to herself, as she finished the

The contents of this letter threw — into flutter of spirits,
in which it was difficult to determine whether pleasure or pain bore the
greatest share. The vague and unsettled suspicions which uncertainty had
produced of what Mr. — might have been doing to forward her sister's
match, which she had feared to encourage, as an exertion of goodness too
great to be probable, and at the same time dreaded to be just, from the

He had followed them purposely to town, he had taken on himself all the
trouble and mortification attendant on such research; in which
supplication had been necessary to woman whom he must abominate and
despise, and where he was reduced to meet, frequently meet, reason with,
persuade, and finally bribe, the man whom he always most wished to
avoid, and whose very name it was punishment to him to pronounce. He had
done all this for girl whom he could neither regard nor esteem. Her
heart did whisper, that he had done it for her. But it was hope
shortly checked by other considerations, and she soon felt that even her
vanity was insufficient, when required to depend on his affection for
her, for woman who had already refused him, as able to overcome
sentiment so natural as abhorrence against relationship with —.
Brother-in-law of —! Every kind of pride must revolt from the
connection. He had to be sure done much. She was ashamed to think how
much. But he had given reason for his interference, which asked no
extraordinary stretch of belief. It was reasonable that he should feel
he had been wrong; he had liberality, and he had the means of exercising
it; and though she would not place herself as his principal inducement,
she could, perhaps, believe, that remaining partiality for her, might
assist his endeavours in cause where her peace of mind must be
materially concerned. It was painful, exceedingly painful, to know that
they were under obligations to person who could never receive
return. They owed the restoration of —, her character, every thing
to him. Oh! how heartily did she grieve over every ungracious sensation
she had ever encouraged, every saucy speech she had ever directed
towards him. For herself she was humbled; but she was proud of him.
Proud that in cause of compassion and honour, he had been able to get
the better of himself. She read over her aunt's commendation of him
again and again. It was hardly enough; but it pleased her. She was even
sensible of some pleasure, though mixed with regret, on finding how
steadfastly both she and her uncle had been persuaded that affection and
confidence subsisted between Mr. — and herself.

—, —, were all forgotten. — was beyond competition her

— petitioned for the use of the library at —; and —

—, from this time, was of course daily visitor at —;

"He has made me so happy," said she, one evening, "by telling me, that

believed it possible."

suspected as much," replied —. "But how did he account for

"It must have been his sister's doing. They were certainly no friends to

"That is the most unforgiving speech," said —, "that ever

"Would you believe it, —, that when he went to town last —,

"He made little mistake to be sure; but it is to the credit of his

This naturally introduced panegyric from — on his diffidence, and

— was pleased to find, that he had not betrayed the interference

am certainly the most fortunate creature that ever existed!" cried
—. "Oh! —, why am thus singled from my family, and blessed

"If you were to give me forty such men, never could be so happy as

The situation of affairs in the — family could not be long

The — were speedily pronounced to be the luckiest family in the

One morning, about week after —'s engagement with — had been

They were of course all intending to be surprised; but their

She entered the room with an air more than usually ungracious, made no

Mrs. — all amazement, though flattered by having guest of such

"Yes, madam," said Mrs. —, delighted to speak to lady —.
"She is my youngest girl but one. My youngest of all, is lately married,

who believe will soon become part of the family."

"You have very small park here," returned — — after short

"It is nothing in comparison of —, my lady, dare say; but
assure you it is much larger than — — —'s."

"This must be most inconvenient sitting room for the evening, in
summer; the windows are full west."

Mrs. — assured her that they never sat there after dinner; and then

"—y take the liberty of asking your ladyship whether you left Mr. and
Mrs. — well."

— now expected that she would produce letter for her from
—, as it seemed the only probable motive for her calling. But no

Mrs. —, with great civility, begged her ladyship to take some

"— —, there seemed to be prettyish kind of little wilderness

Her carriage remained at the door, and — saw that her

"How could ever think her like her nephew?" said she, as she looked in

As soon as they entered the copse, — — began in the following

do not pretend to possess equal frankness with your ladyship. _You_

"Only this; that if he is so, you can have no reason to suppose he will

"The engagement between them is of peculiar kind. From their infancy,

"Let us sit down. You are to understand, — —, that came here

have not been in the habit of brooking disappointment."

"_That_ will make your ladyship's situation at present more pitiable;

will not be interrupted. Hear me in silence. My daughter and my

"True. You _are_ gentleman's daughter. But who was your mother? Who

Though — would not, for the mere purpose of obliging —

— she rose as she spoke. — — rose also, and they turned

have said no such thing. am only resolved to act in that manner,

the world in general would have too much sense to join in

"She is very fine-looking woman! and her calling here was prodigiously

— was forced to give into little falsehood here; for to

The discomposure of spirits, which this extraordinary visit threw

In revolving lady —'s expressions, however, she could not help

probably feel that the arguments, which to — had appeared weak

If he had been wavering before, as to what he should do, which had often
seemed likely, the advice and intreaty of so near relation might
settle every doubt, and determine him at once to be as happy, as dignity
unblemished could make him. In that case he would return no more. —
— might see him in her way through town; and his engagement to
— of coming again to — must give way.

"If, therefore, an excuse for not keeping his promise, should come to
his friend within few days," she added, shall know how to
understand it. shall then give over every expectation, every wish of
his constancy. If he is satisfied with only regretting me, when he might
have obtained my affections and hand, shall soon cease to regret him

The surprise of the rest of the family, on hearing who their visitor had

— was spared from much teazing on the subject.

The next morning, as she was going down stairs, she was met by her
father, who came out of his library with letter in his hand.

She followed him thither; and her curiosity to know what he had to tell

be from lady —; and she anticipated with dismay all the
consequent explanations.

She followed her father to the fire place, and they both sat down. He
then said,

have received letter this morning that has astonished me
exceedingly. As it principally concerns yourself, you ought to know its

The colour now rushed into —'s cheeks in the instantaneous

"You look conscious. Young ladies have great penetration in such matters

"Something very much to the purpose of course. He begins with

"Can you possibly guess, —, who is meant by this?" "This young

"_Mr. —_, you see, is the man! Now, —, think _have_

— tried to join in her father's pleasantry, but could only force

"After mentioning the likelihood of this marriage to her ladyship last

To this question his daughter replied only with laugh; and as it had

They walked towards the —, because — wished to call upon

am sorry, exceedingly sorry," replied —, in tone of surprise

— was too much embarrassed to say word. After short pause,

— feeling all the more than common awkwardness and anxiety of

They walked on, without knowing in what direction. There was too much to

"It taught me to hope," said he, "as had scarcely ever allowed myself

cannot be so easily reconciled to myself. The recollection of what

can easily believe it. You thought me then devoid of every proper

She explained what its effect on her had been, and how gradually all her

knew," said he, "that what wrote must give you pain, but it was

cannot give you credit for any philosophy of the kind. _Your_
