never meant to deceive you, but my spirits might often lead me wrong.

am almost afraid of asking what you thought of me; when we met at

She expressed her gratitude again, but it was too painful subject to

After walking several miles in leisurely manner, and too busy to know

The evening passed quietly, unmarked by any thing extraordinary. The

At night she opened her heart to —. Though suspicion was very far

During their walk, it was resolved that Mr. —'s consent should be

In the evening, soon after Mr. — withdrew to the library, she saw

Her father was walking about the room, looking grave and anxious.
"—," said he, "what are you doing? Are you out of your senses, to be

How earnestly did she then wish that her former opinions had been more

"Or in other words, you are determined to have him. He is rich, to be

do, do like him," she replied, with tears in her eyes, love him.

To complete the favourable impression, she then told him what Mr. —

He then recollected her embarrassment few days before, on his reading

When her mother went up to her dressing-room at night, she followed her,

"Good gracious! — bless me! only think! dear me! Mr. —! Who would

This was sad omen of what her mother's behaviour to the gentleman

admire all my three highly," said he. "—, perhaps,

—'s unjustifiable endeavours to separate us, were the means of

From an unwillingness to confess how much her intimacy with Mr. —

Mr. —'s letter to — —, was in different style; and

must trouble you once more for congratulations. — will

— —'s congratulations to her brother, on his approaching

The joy which — — expressed on receiving similar information, was

Before any answer could arrive from Mr. —, or any congratulations

Mrs. —'s vulgarity was another, and perhaps greater tax on his

too much awe of him to speak with the familiarity which —'s good

Nor was her respect for him, though it made her more quiet, at all

added to the hope of the future; and she looked forward with delight to

Happy for all her maternal feelings was the day on which Mrs. — got
rid of her two most deserving daughters. With what delighted pride she
afterwards visited Mrs. — and talked of Mrs. — may be guessed.
wish could say, for the sake of her family, that the accomplishment

produced so happy an effect as to make her sensible, amiable,
well-informed woman for the rest of her life; though perhaps it was
lucky for her husband, who might not have relished domestic felicity in
so unusual form, that she still was occasionally nervous and
invariably silly.

Mr. — missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her
drew him oftener from home than any thing else could do. He delighted in
going to —, especially when he was least expected.

Mr. — and — remained at — only twelvemonth. So near
vicinity to her mother and — relations was not desirable even to

sisters was then gratified; he bought an estate in neighbouring county

of happiness, were within thirty miles of each other.

—, to her very material advantage, spent the chief of her time with

ignorant, and less insipid. From the farther disadvantage of —'s

balls and young men, her father would never consent to her going.

— was the only daughter who remained at home; and she was necessarily
drawn from the pursuit of accomplishments by Mrs. —'s being quite
unable to sit alone. — was obliged to mix more with the world, but
she could still moralize over every morning visit; and as she was no
longer mortified by comparisons between her sisters' beauty and her own,
it was suspected by her father that she submitted to the change without
much reluctance.

As for — and —, their characters suffered no revolution from
the marriage of her sisters. He bore with philosophy the conviction that
— must now become acquainted with whatever of his ingratitude
and falsehood had before been unknown to her; and in spite of every
thing, was not wholly without hope that — might yet be prevailed on
to make his fortune. The congratulatory letter which — received
from — on her marriage, explained to her that, by his wife at least,
if not by himself, such hope was cherished. The letter was to this
effect:

wish you joy. If you love Mr. — half as well as do my dear
—, you must be very happy. It is great comfort to have you
so rich, and when you have nothing else to do, hope you will
think of us. am sure — would like place at court very
much, and do not think we shall have quite money enough to live
upon without some help. Any place would do, of about three or four
hundred year; but, however, do not speak to Mr. — about it,
if you had rather not.

As it happened that — had _much_ rather not, she endeavoured in
her answer to put an end to every intreaty and expectation of the kind.
Such relief, however, as it was in her power to afford, by the practice
of what might be called economy in her own private expences, she
frequently sent them. It had always been evident to her that such an
income as theirs, under the direction of two persons so extravagant in
their wants, and heedless of the future, must be very insufficient to
their support; and whenever they changed their quarters, either — or
herself were sure of being applied to, for some little assistance
towards discharging their bills. Their manner of living, even when the
restoration of peace dismissed them to home, was unsettled in the
extreme. They were always moving from place to place in quest of cheap
situation, and always spending more than they ought. His affection for
her soon sunk into indifference; her's lasted little longer; and in
spite of her youth and her manners, she retained all the claims to
reputation which her marriage had given her.

Though — could never receive _him_ at —, yet, for
—'s sake, he assisted him farther in his profession. — was
occasionally visitor there, when her husband was gone to enjoy himself
in — or —; and with the — they both of them frequently
staid so long, that even —'s good humour was overcome, and he
proceeded so far as to _talk_ of giving them hint to be gone.

— — was very deeply mortified by —'s marriage; but as she
thought it advisable to retain the right of visiting at —, she
dropt all her resentment; was fonder than ever of —, almost as
attentive to — as heretofore, and paid off every arrear of civility
to —.

— was now —'s home; and the attachment of the sisters
was exactly what — had hoped to see. They were able to love each
other, even as well as they intended. — had the highest opinion
in the world of —; though at first she often listened with an
astonishment bordering on alarm, at her lively, sportive, manner of
talking to her brother. He, who had always inspired in herself respect
which almost overcame her affection, she now saw the object of open
pleasantry. Her mind received knowledge which had never before fallen in
her way. By —'s instructions she began to comprehend that
woman may take liberties with her husband, which brother will not
always allow in sister more than ten years younger than himself.

— — was extremely indignant on the marriage of her nephew;
and as she gave way to all the genuine frankness of her character, in
her reply to the letter which announced its arrangement, she sent him
language so very abusive, especially of —, that for some time
all intercourse was at an end. But at length, by —'s persuasion,
he was prevailed on to overlook the offence, and seek reconciliation;
and, after little farther resistance on the part of his aunt, her
resentment gave way, either to her affection for him, or her curiosity
to see how his wife conducted herself; and she condescended to wait on
them at —, in spite of that pollution which its woods had
received, not merely from the presence of such mistress, but the
visits of her uncle and aunt from the city.

With the —, they were always on the most intimate terms. —,
as well as —, really loved them; and they were both ever
sensible of the warmest gratitude towards the persons who, by bringing
her into —, had been the means of uniting them.
